=== [Bin 9] Sample 53 ===
```python
# Given the premise context, we need to derive the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis.
# The hypothesis is: "Baron is crystal."

# From the premise context, we know:
# 1. If someone is lonely, then he is not important.
# 2. It can be concluded that Baron is crystal and Giles is not important once knowing that Baron is not lonely.
# 3. Someone is not delicious and not vast if and only if he is not important.
# 4. If there is someone who is not vast, then Gabriela is not important and Blair is not lonely.
# 5. As long as someone is important, he is not delicious and not lonely.
# 6. If there is at least one people who is both spotless and not vast, then Blair is not crystal.
# 7. If someone who is not vast is also lonely, then he is delicious.
# 8. If someone is not important or he is spotless, then he is delicious.
# 9. Someone being not vast is equivalent to being not delicious.
# 10. It can be concluded that Giles is not delicious once knowing that Baron is not important or Giles is lonely.
# 11. Abbott is not delicious if and only if Gabriela is spotless and Grover is not important.

# We need to derive the formula for "Baron is crystal".

# Let's break down the steps:
# 1. Assume "Baron is not lonely".
# 2. From the premise, if "Baron is not lonely", then "Baron is crystal".
# 3. Therefore, "Baron is crystal" is true if "Baron is not lonely".

# Now let's write the FOL formula:

# Define the constants
baron = Constant("baron")

# Define the predicate
IsCrystal = Predicate("IsCrystal", [baron])

# Define the assumption
AssumptionNotLonely = Negation(Predicate("Lonely", [baron]))

# Define the conclusion
ConclusionCrystal = IsCrystal

# Combine the assumption and conclusion
Formula = Implication(AssumptionNotLonely, ConclusionCrystal)

# Print the final formula
print(Formula)
```

This code snippet generates the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis based on the provided premise context. The formula states that if "Baron is not lonely," then "Baron is crystal."

=== [Bin 9] Sample 62 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Harold is not shrill.
hypothesis = Negation(Predicate("Shrill", [Constant("harold")]))

# Given premise: Someone who is shrill is both muddy and passionate.
premise_1 = Implication(Predicate("Shrill", [Variable("$x")]), Conjunction(Predicate("Muddy", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Passionate", [Variable("$x")])))

# Given premise: Someone who is not shrill is always impatient.
premise_2 = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Shrill", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Impatient", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If someone is not impatient, then they are not different, and vice versa.
premise_3 = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Impatient", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Different", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# Given premise: If Ramon is different and Seth is not numerous, then Harold is impatient and Ramon is shrill.
premise_4 = Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("Different", [Constant("ramon")]), Negation(Predicate("Numerous", [Constant("seth")])), Conjunction(Predicate("Impatient", [Constant("harold")]), Predicate("Shrill", [Constant("ramon")])))

# Given premise: If there is someone who is not different or not muddy, then Daisy is not numerous and Seth is not passionate.
premise_5 = Implication(Disjunction(Negation(Predicate("Different", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Muddy", [Variable("$x")])), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Numerous", [Constant("daisy")])), Negation(Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("seth")]))

# Given premise: If there is someone who is not impatient, then Harold is different and Harold is passionate.
premise_6 = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Impatient", [Variable("$x")])), Conjunction(Predicate("Different", [Constant("harold")]), Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("harold")]))

# Given premise: If someone is not impatient, then he is not different, and vice versa.
premise_7 = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Impatient", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Different", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# Given premise: If Ramon is not impatient and Harold is passionate, then Milburn is muddy.
premise_8 = Implication(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Impatient", [Constant("ramon")])), Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("harold")])), Predicate("Muddy", [Constant("milburn")]))

# Given premise: If someone is shrill, then he is both muddy and passionate.
premise_9 = Implication(Predicate("Shrill", [Variable("$x")])), Conjunction(Predicate("Muddy", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Passionate", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: Someone is not shrill if and only if he is impatient.
premise_10 = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Shrill", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Impatient", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If Harold is not different, then Milburn is not shrill.
premise_11 = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Different", [Constant("harold")])), Negation(Predicate("Shrill", [Constant("milburn")]))

# Combine all premises and hypothesis
combined_formulas = Conjunction(
    premise_1, premise_2, premise_3, premise_4, premise_5, premise_6, premise_7, premise_8, premise_9, premise_10, premise_11, hypothesis
)

# Final formula
final_formula = combined_formulas
```

=== [Bin 9] Sample 77 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Harold is not shrill.
hypothesis = Negation(Predicate("Shrill", [Constant("harold")]))

# Given premise: If someone is not impatient, then he is not different, and vice versa.
premise = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Impatient", [Constant("x")])), Negation(Predicate("Different", [Constant("x")])))

# Given premise: If someone is shrill, then he is both muddy and passionate.
premise = Implication(Predicate("Shrill", [Constant("x")]), Conjunction(Predicate("Muddy", [Constant("x")]), Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("x")])))

# Given premise: Someone who is not shrill if and only if he is impatient.
premise = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Shrill", [Constant("x")])), Predicate("Impatient", [Constant("x")]))

# Given premise: If Harold is not different, then Milburn is not shrill.
premise = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Different", [Constant("harold")])), Negation(Predicate("Shrill", [Constant("milburn")]))

# Combine all premises and hypothesis into a single formula
combined_formula = Conjunction(
    premise,
    premise,
    premise,
    premise,
    hypothesis
)

# Final formula
End(combined_formula)
```

This code snippet generates the first-order logic formula based on the given premise context and hypothesis. It follows the rules provided for constructing valid FOL expressions.

=== [Bin 9] Sample 126 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Oliver is spotless.
hypothesis = Predicate("Spotless", [Constant("oliver")])

# Given premise: Someone is not enthusiastic if and only if he is not spotless.
# This implies: If someone is enthusiastic, then he is spotless.
# And: If someone is not spotless, then he is enthusiastic.
# We need to find the first-order logic formula for this premise.

# Let's denote:
# E(x): x is enthusiastic
# S(x): x is spotless

# The premise can be written as:
# ∀x (E(x) ↔ S(x))

# Now, let's break it down:
# ∀x (E(x) → S(x)) ∧ ∀x (S(x) → E(x))

# Using the definition of implication:
# ∀x (¬E(x) ∨ S(x)) ∧ ∀x (¬S(x) ∨ E(x))

# Simplifying further:
# ∀x (¬E(x) ∨ S(x)) ∧ ∀x (¬S(x) ∨ E(x))

# This is the first-order logic formula for the given premise.

# Since the hypothesis is Oliver is spotless, we don't need to negate it here.
# We just need to represent the premise in FOL.

# Final FOL formula:
formula = UniversalQuantification(
    Conjunction(
        Implication(Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Spotless", [Variable("$x")])),
        Implication(Predicate("Spotless", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Variable("$x")]))
    ), 
    Variable("$x")
)

End(formula)
```

This is the first-order logic formula for the given premise.

# Given hypothesis: Alan is distinct.
hypothesis = Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("alan")])

# Given premise: There is at least one person who is both enthusiastic and not cruel.
premise1 = ExistentialQuantification(
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Variable("$x")]),
        Negation(Predicate("Cruel", [Variable("$x")])),
    ),
    "$x"
)

# Given premise: If Quentin is brainy, then Kenneth is patient and Oscar is not distinct, and vice versa.
premise2 = Implication(
    Predicate("Brainy", [Constant("quentin")]),
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Patient", [Constant("kenneth")]),
        Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("oscar")])),
    )
)

# Given premise: Someone is not enthusiastic if and only if he is not spotless.
premise3 = Equivalence(
    Negation(Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Variable("$x")])),
    Negation(Predicate("Spotless", [Variable("$x")])),
)

# Given premise: If Landry is not spotless or Kenneth is enthusiastic, then Kenneth is not patient.
premise4 = Implication(
    Disjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Spotless", [Constant("landry")])),
        Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Constant("kenneth")]),
    ),
    Negation(Predicate("Patient", [Constant("kenneth")])),
)

# Given premise: If someone is not enthusiastic, then he is not patient, and vice versa.
premise5 = Equivalence(
    Negation(Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Variable("$x")])),
    Negation(Predicate("Patient", [Variable("$x")])),
)

# Given premise: It can be concluded that Blair is not enthusiastic and Kenneth is not distinct once knowing that Landry is brainy.
premise6 = Implication(
    Predicate("Brainy", [Constant("landry")]),
    Conjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Constant("blair")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("kenneth")])),
    )
)

# Given premise: If there is at least one people who is patient or not enthusiastic, then Kenneth is not cruel.
premise7 = Implication(
    ExistentialQuantification(
        Disjunction(
            Predicate("Patient", [Variable("$x")]),
            Negation(Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Variable("$x")])),
        ),
        "$x"
    ),
    Negation(Predicate("Cruel", [Constant("kenneth")])),
)

# Given premise: It can be concluded that Blair is enthusiastic once knowing that Blair is brainy.
premise8 = Implication(
    Predicate("Brainy", [Constant("blair")]),
    Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Constant("blair")]),
)

# Given premise: If someone is spotless, then he is both not brainy and not distinct.
premise9 = Implication(
    Predicate("Spotless", [Variable("$x")]),
    Conjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Variable("$x")])),
    )
)

# Given premise: If there is at least one people who is distinct or not spotless, then Quentin is not enthusiastic.
premise10 = Implication(
    ExistentialQuantification(
        Disjunction(
            Predicate("Distinct", [Variable("$x")]),
            Negation(Predicate("Spotless", [Variable("$x")])),
        ),
        "$x"
    ),
    Negation(Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Constant("quentin")])),
)

# Given premise: If there is someone who is both enthusiastic and not spotless, then Oliver is patient.
premise11 = Implication(
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Variable("$x")]),
        Negation(Predicate("Spotless", [Variable("$x")])),
    ),
    Predicate("Patient", [Constant("oliver")]),
)

# Given premise: If someone is not enthusiastic and not brainy, then he is both patient and spotless, and vice versa.
premise12 = Equivalence(
    Conjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Variable("$x")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")])),
    ),
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Patient", [Variable("$x")]),
        Predicate("Spotless", [Variable("$x")]),

```python
# Given hypothesis: Alan is brainy.
hypothesis = Predicate("Brainy", [Constant("alan")])

# Given premise: If someone is brainy, then Kenneth is patient and Oscar is not distinct, and vice versa.
premise = Implication(
    Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")]),
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Patient", [Constant("kenneth")]),
        Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("oscar")])),
        Predicate("NotDistinct", [Constant("oscar")])
    )
)

# Since the premise states that if someone is brainy, then Kenneth is patient and Oscar is not distinct, and vice versa,
# we need to consider the contrapositive of this statement to derive the necessary conditions.
contrapositive = Implication(
    Negation(
        Conjunction(
            Predicate("Patient", [Constant("kenneth")]),
            Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("oscar")]))
        )
    ),
    Negation(Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")]))
)

# Now, let's combine the hypothesis and the contrapositive to form the final formula.
final_formula = Implication(
    hypothesis,
    Contrapositive(
        Negation(
            Conjunction(
                Predicate("Patient", [Constant("kenneth")]),
                Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("oscar")]))
            )
        ),
        Negation(Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")]))
    )
)

# Simplify the formula by removing unnecessary parts.
simplified_formula = Implication(
    hypothesis,
    Negation(
        Conjunction(
            Predicate("Patient", [Constant("kenneth")]),
            Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("oscar")]))
        )
    )
)

# Final formula
End(simplified_formula)
```

This formula represents the logical relationship between the hypothesis and the given premise, ensuring that if Alan is brainy, then the conditions involving Kenneth and Oscar hold true.

# Given hypothesis: Clyde is honest.
hypothesis = Predicate("Honest", [Constant("clyde")])

# Given premise: Someone is honest if and only if he is not super.
premise = Equivalence(Predicate("Honest", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Super", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# Generate the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis, given the premise context.
# We need to show that if Clyde is honest, then Clyde is not super.
formula = Implication(hypothesis, Negation(Predicate("Super", [Constant("clyde")])))
End(formula)

```python
# Given hypothesis: Angela is honest.
hypothesis = Predicate("Honest", [Constant("angela")])

# Given premise: Someone is honest if and only if magnus is not super and garret is not impatient.
premise = Equivalence(
    Predicate("Honest", [Variable("$x")]),
    Conjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Super", [Constant("magnus")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Impatient", [Constant("garret")]))
    )
)

# Generate the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis given the premise context.
formula = Implication(premise, hypothesis)

# Print the final formula
print(formula)
```

This code snippet generates the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis given the premise context. It uses the `Equivalence` function to represent the biconditional relationship between being honest and the conditions involving Magnus and Garret. The `Implication` function is used to connect this equivalence to the hypothesis about Angela's honesty.

# Given premise: If there is someone who is either important or not square, then Stacy is muddy.
premise1 = Implication(
    Disjunction(Predicate("Important", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Square", [Variable("$x")]))),
    Predicate("Muddy", [Constant("Stacy")])
)

# Given premise: Someone who is not lazy or not important is always not square and muddy.
premise2 = Implication(
    Disjunction(Negation(Predicate("Lazy", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Important", [Variable("$x")]))),
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Square", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Muddy", [Variable("$x")])))
)

# Given premise: If Kit is lazy, then Kit is not patient and Kit is crystal.
premise3 = Implication(
    Predicate("Lazy", [Constant("Kit")]),
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Patient", [Constant("Kit")])), Predicate("Crystal", [Constant("Kit")]))
)

# Given premise: If there is someone who is both not crystal and important, then Kit is patient.
premise4 = Implication(
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Crystal", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Important", [Variable("$x")])),
    Predicate("Patient", [Constant("Kit")])
)

# Given premise: If someone is patient or muddy, then he is not lazy.
premise5 = Implication(
    Disjunction(Predicate("Patient", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Muddy", [Variable("$x")])),
    Negation(Predicate("Lazy", [Variable("$x")]))
)

# Given premise: If Quenby is not patient and Atwood is not square, then Kenway is muddy.
premise6 = Implication(
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Patient", [Constant("Quenby")])), Negation(Predicate("Square", [Constant("Atwood")]))),
    Predicate("Muddy", [Constant("Kenway")])
)

# Given premise: Someone is important and not square if and only if he is not lazy.
premise7 = Equivalence(
    Conjunction(Predicate("Important", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Square", [Variable("$x")]))),
    Negation(Predicate("Lazy", [Variable("$x")])))
)

# Combine all premises and the hypothesis
combined_formulas = Conjunction(
    Conjunction(
        Conjunction(
            Conjunction(
                Conjunction(
                    Conjunction(
                        Conjunction(
                            Conjunction(
                                Conjunction(
                                    Conjunction(
                                        premise1,
                                        premise2
                                    ),
                                    premise3
                                ),
                                premise4
                            ),
                            premise5
                        ),
                        premise6
                    ),
                    premise7
                ),
                hypothesis
            )
        )
    )
)

# Negate the premise to get the contrapositive.
contrapositive = Implication(Disjunction(Negation(Predicate("Important", [Constant("stacy")])), Negation(Predicate("Muddy", [Constant("stacy")])), Predicate("NotCrystal", [Constant("stacy")]))

# Combine the hypothesis and the contrapositive to form the final FOL formula.
final_formula = Conjunction(hypothesis, contrapositive)

# Print the final FOL formula.
print(final_formula)
```

The hypothesis states that Stacy is crystal, and the premise provides a conditional statement about the properties of someone who is crystal. The contrapositive of this premise is derived, and it is combined with the hypothesis to form the final FOL formula.

# Given hypothesis: Stacy is muddy.

# Given premise: If there is someone who is either important or not square, then Stacy is muddy.
premise_1 = Implication(
    Disjunction(
        Predicate("Important", [Variable("$x")]),
        Negation(Predicate("Square", [Variable("$x")])),
    ),
    Predicate("Muddy", [Constant("stacy")])
)

# Given premise: Someone who is not lazy or not important is always not square and muddy.
premise_2 = Implication(
    Disjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Lazy", [Variable("$x")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Important", [Variable("$x")])),
    ),
    Conjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Square", [Variable("$x")])),
        Predicate("Muddy", [Variable("$x")])
    )
)

final_formula = Conjunction(
    premise_1,
    premise_2,
    hypothesis
)

result = End(final_formula)
print(result)

# Given hypothesis: Roberto is brainy.
hypothesis = Predicate("Brainy", [Constant("roberto")])

# Given premise context:
# 1. Roberto is alive.
# 2. Byron is noisy.
# 3. Magnus is alert.
# 4. Roberto is not alert.
# 5. Harold is not noisy.
# 6. Byron is patient.
# 7. Blair is alive.
# 8. Blair is noisy.
# 9. Casey is not noisy.
# 10. Gabriela is lonely.
# 11. Byron is alert.
# 12. Magnus is not brainy.
# 13. If there is nobody who is not alert, then Casey is alive and Casey is not brainy.
# 14. If there is at least one people who is both not alert and not patient, then Gabriela is brainy.
# 15. Someone being both not lonely and not alert is equivalent to being not brainy.
# 16. Someone who is alert is always lonely.
# 17. If someone is not alive, then he is not lonely, and vice versa.
# 18. If someone is not lonely, then he is alive, and vice versa.
# 19. Harold is not noisy if and only if Casey is not alert and Magnus is not alive.
# 20. Someone being both not brainy and not noisy is equivalent to being not lonely.
# 21. It can be concluded that Gabriela is alert once knowing that Harold is not lonely.
# 22. If someone who is brainy is also not patient, then he is not alert.
# 23. Someone is patient and noisy if and only if he is not lonely and alert.
# 24. If Harold is not brainy or Gabriela is lonely, then Roberto is not noisy.

# Since we need to check if the hypothesis "Roberto is brainy" is consistent with the given premises, we will derive the necessary conditions from the premises and see if they contradict the hypothesis.

# From premise 14: If there is at least one people who is both not alert and not patient, then Gabriela is brainy.
# We know from premise 4 that Roberto is not alert. Let's assume there exists someone who is not alert and not patient. This person could be Casey since Casey is not noisy (premise 9). So, if Casey is not alert and not patient, then Gabriela must be brainy (premise 14).

# From premise 21: It can be concluded that Gabriela is alert once knowing that Harold is not lonely.
# Since Harold is not noisy (premise 5), let's assume Harold is not lonely. Then, Gabriela must be alert (premise 21).

# From premise 22: If someone who is brainy is also not patient, then he is not alert.
# Since we have established that Gabriela is alert (from premise 21), she cannot be brainy (contradiction with premise 22).

# Therefore, the hypothesis "Roberto is brainy" is inconsistent with the given premises.

# The final answer is:
Negation(hypothesis)

# Given hypothesis: Seth is suspicious.
hypothesis = Predicate("Suspicious", [Constant("seth")])

# Given premise context:
# If there is at least one people who is both cruel and not colorful, then Galvin is muddy.
# If someone is colorful and not foreign, then he is both not cruel and not square, and vice versa.
# If Seth is not muddy, then Landon is not square, and vice versa.
# Seth is not muddy if and only if Seth is suspicious and Galvin is foreign.
# Someone who is foreign is always both suspicious and not square.
# If there is someone who is both cruel and suspicious, then Oliver is muddy.
# If someone is foreign or not muddy, then he is square.
# If there is at least one people who is not muddy, then Oliver is not square and Quinby is colorful.
# Landon being not muddy is equivalent to Kenway being not square.
# If there is someone who is cruel, then Galvin is foreign and Nathaniel is muddy.
# As long as someone is not foreign, he is not muddy and not cruel.
# Quinby being not square or Nathaniel being foreign implies that Landon is muddy.

# Let's break down the premises and see how they relate to the hypothesis.

# Since we don't have direct information about Seth being cruel or not, let's consider the implications step-by-step.

# From the premise: "Seth is not muddy if and only if Seth is suspicious and Galvin is foreign."
# This can be written as:
# (¬Suspicious(seth) ↔ (Suspicious(seth) ∧ Foreign(galvin)))

# We know from the hypothesis that Seth is suspicious.
# So, let's substitute Suspicious(seth) with True in the above equation:
# (¬True ↔ (True ∧ Foreign(galvin)))

# Simplifying this, we get:
# False ↔ (True ∧ Foreign(galvin))

# For this to be true, Foreign(galvin) must be False (since False ↔ False).

# Now, let's check if this leads to any contradictions or additional conclusions.

# This can be written as:

# From the premise: "If someone is foreign or not muddy, then he is square."
# This can be written as:
# (Foreign(x) ∨ ¬Muddy(x) → Square(x))

# ¬Muddy(galvin) → Square(galvin)

# This can be written as:
# (∃x ¬Muddy(x) → (¬Square(oliver) ∧ Colorful(quinby)))

# Since we don't have direct information about anyone being not muddy, we cannot conclude anything about Oliver being not square or Quinby being colorful from this premise alone.

# From the premise: "Landon being not muddy is equivalent to Kenway being not square."
# This can be written as:
# (¬Muddy(landon) ↔ ¬Square(kenway))

# Since we don't have direct information about Landon being not muddy, we cannot conclude anything about Kenway being not square from this premise alone.

# From the premise: "If there is someone who is cruel, then Galvin is foreign and Nathaniel is muddy."
# This can be written as:
# (∃x Cruel(x) → (Foreign(galvin) ∧ Muddy(nathaniel)))

# Since we don't have direct information about anyone being cruel, we cannot conclude anything about Galvin being foreign or Nathaniel being muddy from this premise alone.

# From the premise: "As long as someone is not foreign, he is not muddy and not cruel."

# (¬Foreign(x) → (¬Muddy(x) ∧ ¬Cruel(x)))

# From the premise: "Quinby being not square or Nathaniel being foreign implies that Landon is muddy."
# This can be written as:
# ((¬Square(quinby) ∨ Foreign(nathaniel)) → Muddy(landon))

# Since we don't have direct information about Quin

=== [Bin 9] Sample 311 ===
```python
# Given premise: Landon is dramatic.
PremiseContext = [Constant('landon'), Constant('dramatic')]

# Generate the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis.
# Hypothesis: If there is someone who is dramatic, then Landon is not dim and Beatrice is not responsible.
# This can be translated into: ∀x (Dramatic(x) → ¬Dim(Landon) ∧ ¬Responsible(Beatrice))
# Since we know Landon is dramatic from the premise, we need to check if this leads to a contradiction.

# Step-by-step breakdown:
# 1. We know Landon is dramatic.
# 2. According to the hypothesis, if someone is dramatic, then Landon is not dim and Beatrice is not responsible.
# 3. Since Landon is dramatic, the hypothesis implies that Beatrice is not responsible.
# 4. However, we also know from the premise that Beatrice is not responsible.
# 5. The hypothesis does not contradict itself, but it does not provide additional information about Beatrice's responsibility status beyond what is already known.

# Therefore, the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis is:
# ∀x (Dramatic(x) → ¬Dim(Landon) ∧ ¬Responsible(Beatrice))

# Since we know Landon is dramatic, we can simplify this to:
# Dramatic(Landon) → ¬Dim(Landon) ∧ ¬Responsible(Beatrice)

# Which simplifies further to:
# ¬Dim(Landon) ∧ ¬Responsible(Beatrice)

# Since we know Landon is dramatic, the first part of the conjunction is true, so the second part must also be true.
# Therefore, the final answer is:
# ¬Dim(Landon) ∧ ¬Responsible(Beatrice)

# Let's represent this in FOL using the provided functions:
formula = Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Dim", [Constant("landon")])), Negation(Predicate("Responsible", [Constant("beatrice")])))
End(formula)
```

This code snippet generates the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis based on the provided premise context. === [Bin 9] Sample 314 ===
```python
# Given premise: Clayton is worrisome.
# Given premise: Quinby is not responsible.
# Given premise: If there is someone who is grieving, then Beatrice is not worrisome.
# Given premise: If there is someone who is both not relieved and responsible, then Jerome is worrisome.
# Given premise: Beatrice is not worrisome if and only if Landon is dramatic and Beatrice is not responsible.
# Given premise: Someone being responsible is equivalent to being not grieving.
# Given premise: Someone who is both not relieved and worrisome is always not dim.
# Given premise: Someone who is not relieved and dim if and only if he is grieving.
# Given premise: If someone is both grieving and worrisome, then he is dramatic.
# Given premise: If someone is responsible and grieving, then he is dramatic, and vice versa.
# Given premise: If there is someone who is both relieved and not dramatic, then Benjamin is not grieving and Jerome is worrisome.
# Given premise: Someone who is not worrisome and not relieved is always not grieving and dramatic.
# Given premise: Clayton is worrisome if and only if Quinby is not responsible.
# Given premise: If there is someone who is grieving, then Beatrice is not worrisome.

# Hypothesis: Landon is not dim.

# Step-by-step derivation:
# 1. From the premise "Clayton is worrisome," we know that Quinby is not responsible.
# 2. From the premise "Clayton is worrisome if and only if Quinby is not responsible," this confirms our step 1.
# 3. From the premise "If there is someone who is grieving, then Beatrice is not worrisome," we need to consider if anyone is grieving.
# 4. Since we don't have direct information about anyone being grieving, we cannot definitively conclude anything about Beatrice's state.
# 5. However, we can use the premise "If there is someone who is both not relieved and responsible, then Jerome is worrisome."
# 6. We also know from the premise "Someone being responsible is equivalent to being not grieving." This means if someone is responsible, they are not grieving.
# 7. From the premise "Someone who is not relieved and dim if and only if he is grieving," we know that if someone is not relieved and dim, they must be grieving.
# 8. From the premise "If someone is both grieving and worrisome, then he is dramatic," and since we don't have anyone being worrisome, this doesn't apply.
# 9. From the premise "If someone is responsible and grieving, then he is dramatic, and vice versa," and since we don't have anyone being responsible, this doesn't apply.
# 10. From the premise "If there is someone who is both relieved and not dramatic, then Benjamin is not grieving and Jerome is worrisome," and since we don't have anyone being relieved, this doesn't apply.
# 11. From the premise "Someone who is not worrisome and not relieved is always not grieving and dramatic," and since we don't have anyone being not worrisome and not relieved, this doesn't apply.
# 12. Since we don't have any direct evidence that Landon is dim or not dim, and we don't have any evidence that anyone is grieving, we cannot determine whether Landon is not dim based on the given premises.

# Conclusion: The hypothesis "Landon is not dim" cannot be derived from the given premises without additional information.

# Final answer: Paradox
```

# Given hypothesis: Clayton is passionate.
hypothesis = Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("clayton")])

# Given premise: Someone who is eager is always passionate.

# Given premise: If Clayton is passionate, then Bartholomew is not rich.
premise2 = Implication(Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("clayton")]), Negation(Predicate("Rich", [Constant("bartholomew")]))) 

# Given premise: If there is at least one person who is eager, then Landon is not rich and Garret is colorful.
premise3 = Implication(ExistentialQuantification(Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")]), "x"), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Rich", [Constant("landon")])), Predicate("Colorful", [Constant("garret")]))) 

# Given premise: If Clayton is not vast, then Medwin is not passionate.
premise4 = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Vast", [Constant("clayton")])), Negation(Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("medwin")]))) 

# Given premise: If there is at least one person who is enthusiastic, then Bartholomew is not passionate and Medwin is colorful.
premise5 = Implication(ExistentialQuantification(Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Variable("$x")]), "x"), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("bartholomew")])), Predicate("Colorful", [Constant("medwin")]))) 

# Given premise: If there is at least one person who is eager, then Landon is not rich and Garret is colorful.
premise6 = Implication(ExistentialQuantification(Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")]), "x"), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Rich", [Constant("landon")])), Predicate("Colorful", [Constant("garret")]))) 

# Given premise: If Clayton is passionate and Bartholomew is not rich, then Garret is vast.
premise7 = Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("clayton")]), Negation(Predicate("Rich", [Constant("bartholomew")])), Predicate("Vast", [Constant("garret")]))) 

# Combine all premises and the hypothesis
combined_formulas = Conjunction(
    premise1,
    premise2,
    premise3,
    premise4,
    premise5,
    premise6,
    premise7,
    hypothesis
)

# Final formula
final_formula = combined_formulas

# Print the final formula
print(final_formula)
```

This script generates the first-order logic formula based on the given premise context and hypothesis. It uses the functions provided to construct the necessary logical expressions, ensuring that all rules are followed. The final formula combines all the premises and the hypothesis into a single conjunction.

```python
# Given hypothesis: Garret is enthusiastic.
hypothesis = Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Constant("garret")])

# Given premise: Someone who is eager is always passionate.
premise1 = Implication(Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Passionate", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If all people are not eager, then Clayton is not vast.
premise2 = Implication(UniversalQuantification(Negation(Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")])), "x"), Negation(Predicate("Vast", [Constant("clayton")]))) 

# Given premise: If Clayton is not vast, then Medwin is not passionate.
premise3 = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Vast", [Constant("clayton")])), Negation(Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("medwin")]))) 

# Given premise: If there is at least one person who is eager, then Landon is not rich and Garret is colorful.
premise4 = Implication(ExistentialQuantification(Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")]), "x"), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Rich", [Constant("landon")])), Predicate("Colorful", [Constant("garret")]))) 

# Given premise: Landon being not vast or Archer being not eager implies that Archer is colorful.
premise5 = Implication(Disjunction(Negation(Predicate("Vast", [Constant("landon")])), Negation(Predicate("Eager", [Constant("archer")])), Predicate("Colorful", [Constant("archer")]))) 

# Given premise: Someone who is either enthusiastic or rich is always not colorful.
premise6 = Implication(Disjunction(Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Rich", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Colorful", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# Given premise: Someone is not rich if and only if he is eager.
premise7 = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Rich", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# Given premise: If there is at least one person who is enthusiastic, then Bartholomew is not passionate and Medwin is colorful.
premise8 = Implication(ExistentialQuantification(Predicate("Enthusiastic", [Variable("$x")]), "x"), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("bartholomew")])), Predicate("Colorful", [Constant("medwin")]))) 

# Combine all premises and the hypothesis
combined_formulas = Conjunction(
    premise1,
    premise2,
    premise3,
    premise4,
    premise5,
    premise6,
    premise7,
    premise8,
    hypothesis
)

# Final formula
final_formula = End(combined_formulas)
print(final_formula)
```

This code generates the first-order logic formula based on the given premise context and hypothesis. It uses the functions provided to construct the necessary logical expressions, ensuring that all rules are followed.

    Predicate("Weary", [Constant("vivian")]),
    Implication(ExistentialQuantification(Disjunction(Predicate("NotSorry", [Variable("x")]), Predicate("Poor", [Variable("x")])), "x"), Predicate("Weary", [Constant("adley")])),
    Implication(Predicate("Weary", [Constant("quentin")]), Negation(Predicate("Poor", [Constant("nick")]))),
    Implication(Disjunction(Negation(Predicate("Obvious", [Variable("x")])), Negation(Predicate("NotDifferent", [Variable("x")])), Predicate("NotSorry", [Variable("x")])),
    Implication(ExistentialQuantification(Conjunction(Predicate("Calm", [Variable("x")]), Negation(Predicate("Weary", [Variable("x")])), "x"), Predicate("NotObvious", [Constant("quentin")]), Predicate("Different", [Constant("vivian")])),
    Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("Calm", [Constant("jaime")]), Negation(Predicate("Weary", [Constant("quentin")])), Predicate("NotDifferent", [Constant("nick")])),
    Implication(Predicate("NotSorry", [Constant("vivian")]), Conjunction(Predicate("Different", [Constant("kit")]), Negation(Predicate("Obvious", [Constant("quentin")])),
    Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("Obvious", [Constant("jaime")]), Negation(Predicate("Calm", [Constant("quentin")])), Predicate("NotDifferent", [Constant("nick")])),
    Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("NotDifferent", [Variable("x")]), Predicate("Weary", [Variable("x")])), Predicate("Poor", [Variable("x")])),
    Implication(ExistentialQuantification(Predicate("Different", [Variable("x")]), "x"), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Calm", [Constant("jaime")])), Predicate("Sorry", [Constant("kit")])))
]

# Combine all premises and hypothesis
final_formula = Conjunction(*premise_context, hypothesis)

# Given hypothesis: Clayton is fancy.
hypothesis = Predicate("Fancy", [Constant("clayton")])

# Given premise: If someone is fancy, they are gorgeous and not financial.
premise = Implication(Predicate("Fancy", [Variable("$x")]), Conjunction(Predicate("Gorgeous", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Financial", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# Combine the hypothesis and the premise to form the final formula.
final_formula = Implication(premise, hypothesis)

# Wrap the final formula in End to indicate the end of the formula.
End(final_formula)

=== [Bin 9] Sample 553 ===
```python
# Given Hypothesis: Oscar is fancy.
hypothesis = Predicate("Fancy", [Constant("oscar")])

# Premises:
# 1. Oscar is actual.
actual_oscar = Predicate("Actual", [Constant("oscar")])
# 2. Someone being both gorgeous and not fancy is equivalent to being not bad-tempered and not actual.
gorgeous_not_fancy_equivalent = Equivalence(
    Conjunction(Predicate("Gorgeous", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Fancy", [Variable("$x")]))),
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("BadTempered", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Actual", [Variable("$x")])))
)
# 3. If Oscar is actual, then Oscar is not bad-tempered.
actual_implies_not_bad_tempered = Implication(actual_oscar, Negation(Predicate("BadTempered", [Constant("oscar")])))
# 4. If Oscar is not bad-tempered, then Oscar is not actual.
not_bad_tempered_implies_not_actual = Implication(Negation(Predicate("BadTempered", [Constant("oscar")])), Negation(actual_oscar))
# 5. Oscar is not bad-tempered and not actual.
oscar_not_bad_tempered_and_not_actual = Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("BadTempered", [Constant("oscar")])), Negation(actual_oscar))

# Combining all premises to form the final formula
final_formula = Conjunction(
    actual_oscar,
    gorgeos_not_fancy_equivalent,
    actual_implies_not_bad_tempered,
    not_bad_tempered_implies_not_actual,
    oscar_not_bad_tempered_and_not_actual
)

# Adding the hypothesis to the final formula
final_formula_with_hypothesis = Conjunction(final_formula, hypothesis)

# The final first-order logic formula
End(final_formula_with_hypothesis)
```

This code snippet generates the first-order logic formula based on the given premise context and hypothesis. It uses the functions provided to construct the necessary logical expressions and combines them according to the rules specified.

```python
# Given hypothesis: Byron is not capable
hypothesis = Predicate("NotCapable", [Constant("byron")])

# Given premise: If someone is not alive, then he is both sorry and not pure.
premise1 = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Alive", [Constant("someone")])), Conjunction(Predicate("Sorry", [Constant("someone")]), Predicate("NotPure", [Constant("someone")])))

# Given premise: If there is someone who is sorry, then Abbott is alive.
premise2 = Implication(Predicate("Sorry", [Constant("someone")]), Predicate("Alive", [Constant("abbott")]))

# Given premise: It can be concluded that Abbott is bossy once knowing that Abbott is alive.
premise3 = Implication(Predicate("Alive", [Constant("abbott")]), Predicate("Bossy", [Constant("abbott")]))

# Given premise: If there is someone who is either capable or not bitter, then Byron is not pure.
premise4 = Implication(Disjunction(Predicate("Capable", [Constant("someone")]), Predicate("NotBitter", [Constant("someone")])), Predicate("NotPure", [Constant("byron")]))

# Given premise: Someone who is capable is always not pure.
premise5 = Implication(Predicate("Capable", [Constant("someone")]), Predicate("NotPure", [Constant("someone")]))

# Given premise: If someone is bossy and not bitter, then he is both not pure and not sorry, and vice versa.
premise6 = Equivalence(Conjunction(Predicate("Bossy", [Constant("someone")]), Predicate("NotBitter", [Constant("someone")])), Conjunction(Predicate("NotPure", [Constant("someone")]), Predicate("NotSorry", [Constant("someone")]))) 

# Given premise: If someone is not bossy and alive, then he is not pure, and vice versa.
premise7 = Equivalence(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Bossy", [Constant("someone")])), Predicate("Alive", [Constant("someone")])), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Pure", [Constant("someone")])), Predicate("NotAlive", [Constant("someone")]))) 

# Given premise: Daisy being not alive implies that Daisy is bossy.
premise8 = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Alive", [Constant("daisy")])), Predicate("Bossy", [Constant("daisy")]))

# Given premise: If Angela is bitter, then Atwood is not alive and Nicole is sorry, and vice versa.
premise9 = Equivalence(Predicate("Bitter", [Constant("angela")]), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Alive", [Constant("atwood")])), Predicate("Sorry", [Constant("nicole")]))) 

# Given premise: It can be concluded that Byron is not capable and Atwood is not sorry once knowing that Nicole is pure.
premise10 = Implication(Predicate("Pure", [Constant("nicole")]), Conjunction(Predicate("NotCapable", [Constant("byron")]), Negation(Predicate("Sorry", [Constant("atwood")]))) 

# Given premise: If someone is pure and sorry, then he is not bossy, and vice versa.
premise11 = Equivalence(Conjunction(Predicate("Pure", [Constant("someone")]), Predicate("Sorry", [Constant("someone")])), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Bossy", [Constant("someone")])), Negation(Predicate("NotSorry", [Constant("someone")]))) 

# Combine all premises and hypothesis into a single formula
combined_formula = Conjunction(
    premise1, premise2, premise3, premise4, premise5, premise6, premise7, premise8, premise9, premise10, premise11, hypothesis
)

# Final formula
formula = End(combined_formula)
```

=== [Bin 9] Sample 685 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Hugh is kind.
hughIsKind = Predicate("Kind", [Constant("Hugh")])

# Given premise: Hugh is not nervous.
hughIsNotNervous = Predicate("NotNervous", [Constant("Hugh")])

# Given premise: Hugh is scared.
hughIsScared = Predicate("Scared", [Constant("Hugh")])

# Given premise: If someone is both tasty and super, then he is nervous.
tastyAndSuperImpliesNervous = Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("Tasty", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Super", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Nervous", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If someone is grieving, then he is kind.
grievingImpliesKind = Implication(Predicate("Grieving", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Kind", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: Julian is not grieving if and only if Hugh is not nervous.
julianNotGrievingIffHughNotNervous = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Grieving", [Constant("Julian")])), Negation(Predicate("Nervous", [Constant("Hugh")]))

# Given premise: If someone is not super, then he is both kind and tasty.
notSuperImpliesKindAndTasty = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Super", [Variable("$x")])), Conjunction(Predicate("Kind", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Tasty", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If someone who is tasty is also scared, then he is grieving.
tastyAndScaredImpliesGrieving = Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("Tasty", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Scared", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Grieving", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If everyone is super or tasty, then Monroe is not nervous.
everyoneSuperOrTastyImpliesMonroeNotNervous = Implication(UniversalQuantification(Disjunction(Predicate("Super", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Tasty", [Variable("$x")])), "x"), Negation(Predicate("Nervous", [Constant("Monroe")]))

# Given premise: Someone is kind and grieving if and only if he is not nervous and not super.
kindAndGrievingIffNotNervousAndNotSuper = Equivalence(Conjunction(Predicate("Kind", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Grieving", [Variable("$x")])), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Nervous", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Super", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If someone is not tasty and super, then he is not scared and kind.
notTastyAndSuperImpliesNotScaredAndKind = Implication(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Tasty", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Super", [Variable("$x")])), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Scared", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Kind", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: Hugh is not nervous if and only if Hugh is not scared.
hughNotNervousIffNotScared = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Nervous", [Constant("Hugh")])), Negation(Predicate("Scared", [Constant("Hugh")]))

# Combine all premises and hypothesis into a single formula
combinedFormula = Conjunction(
    hughIsKind,
    hughIsNotNervous,
    hughIsScared,
    tastyAndSuperImpliesNervous,
    grievingImpliesKind,
    julianNotGrievingIffHughNotNervous,
    notSuperImpliesKindAndTasty,
    tastyAndScaredImpliesGrieving,
    everyoneSuperOrTastyImpliesMonroeNotNervous,
    kindAndGrievingIffNotNervousAndNotSuper,
    notTastyAndSuperImpliesNotScaredAndKind,
    hughNotNervousIffNotScared
)

# Final formula
finalFormula = combinedFormula
```

# Given hypothesis: Adley is intelligent.
hypothesis = Predicate("Intelligent", [Constant("adley")])

# Given premises:
# 1. Someone who is dramatic or intelligent is always not cheerful and not doubtful.
premise1 = Implication(Disjunction(Predicate("Dramatic", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Intelligent", [Variable("$x")])), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Cheerful", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Doubtful", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# 2. If Alden is cheerful, then Adley is doubtful and Victor is not embarrassed, and vice versa.
premise2 = Equivalence(Predicate("Cheerful", [Constant("alden")]), Conjunction(Predicate("Doubtful", [Constant("adley")]), Negation(Predicate("Embarrassed", [Constant("victor")])))) 

# 3. If Carrick is not cheerful and Alden is not embarrassed, then Adley is not intelligent.

# 4. As long as someone is not intelligent and doubtful, he is delicious and not dramatic.
premise4 = Implication(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Intelligent", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Doubtful", [Variable("$x")])), Conjunction(Predicate("Delicious", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Dramatic", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# 5. Adley is not intelligent if and only if Alden is cheerful.
premise5 = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Intelligent", [Constant("adley")])), Predicate("Cheerful", [Constant("alden")]))

# Combine all premises and hypothesis
combined_formulas = Conjunction(premise1, premise2, premise3, premise4, premise5, hypothesis)

# Final formula
final_formula = combined_formulas

```python
# Given premise: Alden is dramatic.
# According to the rules, we need to decompose this statement into a predicate.
# The predicate should have a short and general name, and it should follow the decomposition rule.
# Here, "Dramatic" can be decomposed into "IsDramatic".
# The subject of the predicate is "Alden".

# Decomposing the premise into a predicate
predicate_name = "IsDramatic"
subject = Constant("alden")
formula = Predicate(predicate_name, [subject])

# Since the premise is a simple statement without any additional conditions or implications,
# we don't need to add any further logical operations or quantifiers.

# Final formula representing the premise
End(formula)
```

```python
# Given hypothesis: Clyde is eager and Carver is real once knowing that Simon is not distinct.
# We need to derive this from the given premises.

# Step-by-step derivation:

# Premise: If there is someone who is not distinct, then Carver is dangerous.
# This can be written as: ∀x (NotDistinct(x) → Dangerous(Carver))

# Premise: If Simon is not distinct, then Clyde is eager.
# This can be written as: NotDistinct(Simon) → Eager(Clyde)

# Premise: If Clyde is eager, then Carver is real.
# This can be written as: Eager(Clyde) → Real(Carver)

# Combining these premises, we get:
# NotDistinct(Simon) → Eager(Clyde) → Real(Carver)

# Since we know that Simon is not distinct, we can substitute NotDistinct(Simon) with True:
# True → Eager(Clyde) → Real(Carver)

# By modus ponens, we can conclude:
# Eager(Clyde) → Real(Carver)

# Now, we need to show that if Clyde is eager, then Carver is real.
# From the premises, we already have:
# Eager(Clyde) → Real(Carver)

# Therefore, the hypothesis Clyde is eager and Carver is real once knowing that Simon is not distinct is true.

# The final FOL formula for the hypothesis is:
End(Conjunction(Eager(Clyde), Real(Carver)))
```

# Given Hypothesis: Clyde is eager and Carver is real once knowing that Simon is not distinct.
# Premise Context: Various statements about individuals and their properties.

# Step-by-step derivation:

# 1. From the premise context, we know:
#    - Simon is not distinct.
#    - If Simon is not distinct, then Carver is not relieved, and vice versa.
#    - If Carver is not relieved, then Bradley is dangerous and Carver is not eager.
#    - If Carver is not eager, then Carver is real.

# 2. Since Simon is not distinct, according to the premise context, Carver is not relieved.
# 3. If Carver is not relieved, then Bradley is dangerous and Carver is not eager.
# 4. If Carver is not eager, then Carver is real.

# Combining these steps, we get:
# If Simon is not distinct, then Carver is not eager, and if Carver is not eager, then Carver is real.
# This simplifies to: If Simon is not distinct, then Carver is real.

# Additionally, we need to show that Clyde is eager under the same condition.

# 5. From the premise context, we know:
#    - If there is someone who is not distinct, then Carver is dangerous.
#    - If Carver is dangerous, then Clyde is real.
#    - If Clyde is real, then Clyde is eager.

# Combining these steps, we get:
# If there is someone who is not distinct, then Clyde is eager.

# Since Simon is not distinct, it follows that Clyde is eager.

# Final formula:
ExistentialQuantification(
    Implication(
        Predicate("NotDistinct", [Constant("simon")]),
        Conjunction(
            Predicate("Real", [Constant("carver")]),
            Predicate("Eager", [Constant("clyde")])
        )
    ), "x"
)
```

This formula represents the first-order logic statement derived from the given premise context and hypothesis. It ensures that Clyde is eager and Carver is real when Simon is not distinct, adhering to the rules and constraints provided.

```python
# Given hypothesis: Clyde is eager and Carver is real
hypothesis = Conjunction(Predicate("Eager", [Constant("clyde")]), Predicate("Real", [Constant("carver")]))

# Given premise context
premise_context = [
    Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("carver")]),
    Negation(Predicate("Dangerous", [Constant("carver")])),
    Negation(Predicate("Dangerous", [Constant("adley")])),
    Predicate("Proud", [Constant("malcolm")]),
    Negation(Predicate("Relieved", [Constant("bradley")])),
    Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("simon")])),
    Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("bradley")]),
    Negation(Predicate("Dangerous", [Constant("simon")])),
    Negation(Predicate("Real", [Constant("bradley")])),
    Negation(Predicate("Dangerous", [Constant("clyde")])),
    Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("adley")]),
    Negation(Predicate("Proud", [Constant("bradley")])),
    Implication(Disjunction(Negation(Predicate("Relieved", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotRelieved", [Variable("$x")])), Conjunction(Predicate("Dangerous", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotEager", [Variable("$x")])), "x"),
    Implication(Disjunction(Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotDistinct", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotEager", [Variable("$x")])), "x"),
    Equivalence(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Dangerous", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotProud", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotDistinct", [Variable("$x")])),
    Implication(Disjunction(Predicate("Dangerous", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Distinct", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")])), "x"),
    Implication(Negation(Predicate("Proud", [Constant("simon")])), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Eager", [Constant("malcolm")])), Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("adley")])), "x"),
    Implication(Negation(Predicate("Proud", [Constant("malcolm")])), Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("malcolm")])), "x"),
    Implication(Disjunction(Predicate("Real", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Proud", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotProud", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotDistinct", [Variable("$x")])), "x"),
    Implication(Disjunction(Predicate("NotDistinct", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotEager", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Relieved", [Variable("$x")])), "x"),
    Equivalence(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Dangerous", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotProud", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotDistinct", [Variable("$x")])),
]

# Check if the hypothesis is entailed by the premise context
is_entailed = True
for premise in premise_context:
    if not Implication(premise, hypothesis).simplify():
        is_entailed = False
        break

if is_entailed:
    print(f"The hypothesis '{hypothesis}' is entailed by the premise context.")
else:
    print(f"The hypothesis '{hypothesis}' is not entailed by the premise context.")
```

This code checks whether the given hypothesis is entailed by the provided premise context. It iterates through each premise and checks if the implication from the premise to the hypothesis holds true. If any premise does not imply the hypothesis, it concludes that the hypothesis is not entailed by the premise context.

=== [Bin 9] Sample 767 ===
```python
# Given the premise context, we need to derive the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis:
# "Skyla is rich once knowing that Beatrice is not modest or Kenway is responsible."

# Step-by-step derivation:

# 1. **Premise Analysis**:
   - Skyla is not modest.
   - Beatrice is not rich.
   - Seth is not responsible.
   - Seth is filthy.
   - Quenby is not filthy.
   - Quenby is not inexpensive.
   - Kenway is rich.
   - Hale is filthy.
   - Alden is inexpensive.
   - Seth is rich.
   - Kenway is not modest.
   - Quenby is modest.
   
   - Conclusion: If Beatrice is not modest or Kenway is responsible, then Skyla is rich.

# 2. **Formulating the Hypothesis**:
   - Let's denote:
     - `SkylaRich` as Skyla is rich.
     - `BeatriceNotModest` as Beatrice is not modest.
     - `KenwayResponsible` as Kenway is responsible.
     
   - The hypothesis can be written as:
     \[
     (\text{BeatriceNotModest} \lor \text{KenwayResponsible}) \rightarrow \text{SkylaRich}
     \]

# 3. **Constructing the Formula**:
   - Using the functions provided, we construct the formula as follows:

```python
from typing import List

# Define constants
skylaRich = Constant('SkylaRich')
beatriceNotModest = Constant('BeatriceNotModest')
kenwayResponsible = Constant('KenwayResponsible')

# Construct the hypothesis formula
hypothesis_formula = Implication(
    Disjunction(beatriceNotModest, kenwayResponsible),
    skylaRich
)

# Print the final formula
print(hypothesis_formula)
```

This will output the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis based on the provided premise context.

# Given premises and hypothesis
# Premises:
# 1. Skyla is not modest.
# 2. Beatrice is not rich.
# 3. Seth is not responsible.
# 4. Seth is filthy.
# 5. Quenby is not filthy.
# 6. Quenby is not inexpensive.
# 7. Kenway is rich.
# 8. Hale is filthy.
# 9. Alden is inexpensive.
# 10. Seth is rich.
# 11. Kenway is not modest.

condition_2 = Predicate("Responsible", [Constant("kenway")])

conclusion = Implication(Disjunction(condition_1, condition_2), hypothesis)

# Final formula
formula = End(conclusion)

=== [Bin 9] Sample 797 ===
```python
# Given the premise context, we need to derive the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis.
# The hypothesis is: "Jaime is not tasty."

# From the premise context, we know:
# - If someone is distinct or nasty, then they are delicious.
# - If someone is not tasty, then they are shy.
# - All distinct people are nasty.
# - Someone being both distinct and not delicious is equivalent to being nasty.

# Let's break down the steps to derive the formula:

# Step 1: Identify the relevant premises
# - Premise 1: If someone is distinct or nasty, then they are delicious.
# - Premise 2: If someone is not tasty, then they are shy.
# - Premise 3: All distinct people are nasty.
# - Premise 4: Someone being both distinct and not delicious is equivalent to being nasty.

# Step 2: Use the premises to derive the formula for "Jaime is not tasty"
# Since Jaime is distinct (from the premise context), let's use this information to derive the formula.

# From Premise 3: All distinct people are nasty.
# This means if Jaime is distinct, then Jaime is nasty.
# So, we can write: Distinct(jaime) → Nasty(jaime).

# From Premise 1: If someone is distinct or nasty, then they are delicious.
# This means if Jaime is distinct or Jaime is nasty, then Jaime is delicious.
# So, we can write: (Distinct(jaime) ∨ Nasty(jaime)) → Delicious(jaime).

# Since we know from Premise 3 that Jaime is nasty if Jaime is distinct, we can substitute Nasty(jaime) for Distinct(jaime) in the above formula.
# So, we get: (Nasty(jaime) ∨ Nasty(jaime)) → Delicious(jaime).
# Simplifying this, we get: Nasty(jaime) → Delicious(jaime).

# Now, we need to find the contrapositive of this statement to derive the formula for "Jaime is not tasty".
# The contrapositive of "Nasty(jaime) → Delicious(jaime)" is "NotDelicious(jaime) → NotNasty(jaime)".
# But since we are looking for "Jaime is not tasty", we need to use the fact that if someone is not tasty, then they are shy.
# So, we can write: NotTasty(jaime) → Shy(jaime).

# Combining all the steps, we get the final formula:
# NotTasty(jaime) → Shy(jaime).

# Using the functions provided, we can write the formula as follows:
formula = End(Implies(NotTasty('jaime'), Shy('jaime')))
```

This is the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis based on the provided premise context.

=== [Bin 9] Sample 915 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Lucas is not nasty
hypothesis = Predicate("NotNasty", [Constant("Lucas")])

# Given premise: If Conner is not gorgeous, then Seth is not blue and Grover is not lonely.
premise1 = Implication(
    Negation(Predicate("Gorgeous", [Constant("Conner")])),
    Conjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Blue", [Constant("Seth")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Lonely", [Constant("Grover")]))
    )
)

# Given premise: If someone is not difficult and not delicious, then he is both not nasty and not gorgeous, and vice versa.
premise2 = Equivalence(
    Conjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Difficult", [Variable("$x")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Delicious", [Variable("$x")]))
    ),
    Conjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Nasty", [Variable("$x")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Gorgeous", [Variable("$x")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Difficult", [Variable("$x")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Delicious", [Variable("$x")])),
        Predicate("Gorgeous", [Variable("$x")]),
        Predicate("Delicious", [Variable("$x")])
    )
)

# Given premise: If someone is not gorgeous or he is not lonely, then he is nasty.
premise3 = Implication(
    Disjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Gorgeous", [Variable("$x")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Lonely", [Variable("$x")])),
    ),
    Predicate("Nasty", [Variable("$x")])
)

# Given premise: Lucas being difficult is equivalent to Toby being not delicious and Oswald being not blue.
premise4 = Equivalence(
    Predicate("Difficult", [Constant("Lucas")]),
    Conjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Delicious", [Constant("Toby")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Blue", [Constant("Oswald")]))
    )
)

# Given premise: Someone who is not nasty is always both gorgeous and delicious.
premise5 = Implication(
    Negation(Predicate("Nasty", [Variable("$x")])),
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Gorgeous", [Variable("$x")]),
        Predicate("Delicious", [Variable("$x")])
    )
)

# Given premise: If there is someone who is not difficult, then Conner is not lonely.
premise6 = Implication(
    Negation(Predicate("Difficult", [Variable("$x")])),
    Negation(Predicate("Lonely", [Constant("Conner")]))
)

# Given premise: If someone is nasty or he is lonely, then he is not delicious.
premise7 = Implication(
    Disjunction(
        Predicate("Nasty", [Variable("$x")]),
        Predicate("Lonely", [Variable("$x")]),
    ),
    Negation(Predicate("Delicious", [Variable("$x")]))
)

# Given premise: If there is at least one people who is gorgeous, then Toby is difficult and Lucas is not nasty.
premise8 = Implication(
    ExistentialQuantification(
        Predicate("Gorgeous", [Variable("$x")]), 
        Variable("$x")
    ),
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Difficult", [Constant("Toby")]),
        Negation(Predicate("Nasty", [Constant("Lucas")]))
    )
)

# Given premise: As long as someone is delicious and lonely, he is gorgeous and not blue.
premise9 = Implication(
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Delicious", [Variable("$x")]),
        Predicate("Lonely", [Variable("$x")]),
    ),
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Gorgeous", [Variable("$x")]),
        Negation(Predicate("Blue", [Variable("$x")]))
    )
)

# Combine all premises and the hypothesis
combined_formulas = Conjunction(
    premise1,
    premise2,
    premise3,
    premise4,
    premise5,
    premise6,
    premise7,
    premise8,
    premise9,
    hypothesis
)

# Final formula
final_formula = combined_formulas
```

=== [Bin 9] Sample 919 ===
```python
# Given the premise context, we need to derive the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis.
# The hypothesis is: "Lucas is not nasty."

# From the premise context, we have:
# Lucas being difficult is equivalent to Toby being not delicious and Oswald being not blue.
# Lucas being difficult is equivalent to Toby being not delicious and Oswald being not blue.
# Lucas being difficult is equivalent to Toby being not delicious and Oswald being not blue.

# We can represent this relationship as:
# Difficult(Lucas) ↔ (NotDelicious(Toby) ∧ NotBlue(Oswald))

# Since Lucas being difficult is equivalent to Toby being not delicious and Oswald being not blue,
# if Lucas is not difficult, then Toby must be delicious and Oswald must be blue.
# This can be represented as:
# ¬Difficult(Lucas) → (Delicious(Toby) ∧ Blue(Oswald))

# Now, we need to find the formula for Lucas being not nasty.
# From the premise context, if someone is not nasty, they are always both gorgeous and delicious.
# This can be represented as:
# ¬Nasty(x) → (Gorgeous(x) ∧ Delicious(x))

# Since Lucas is not difficult, Toby must be delicious and Oswald must be blue.
# And since Lucas is not nasty, he must be gorgeous and delicious.
# Therefore, Lucas is not nasty.

# The formula for Lucas being not nasty is:
Negation(Predicate("Nasty", [Constant("Lucas")]))
```

This is the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis based on the provided premise context.

```python
# Given hypothesis: Arnold is not brainy.
hypothesis = Negation(Predicate("Brainy", [Constant("arnold")]))

# Given premise: Arnold is not tiny.
premise = Negation(Predicate("Tiny", [Constant("arnold")]))

# Given premise: If someone is distinct, then he is not tiny, and vice versa.
distinct_and_not_tiny = Implication(Predicate("Distinct", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Tiny", [Variable("$x")])))
not_distinct_or_tiny = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Tiny", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If someone is distinct, then he is both not capable and brainy.
distinct_and_not_capable_and_brainy = Implication(Predicate("Distinct", [Variable("$x")]), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Capable", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: Someone being both not dangerous and not brainy is equivalent to being not capable.
not_dangerous_and_not_brainy_equivalent_to_not_capable = Equivalence(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Dangerous", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotCapable", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: As long as someone is not tiny, he is not distinct and brainy.
not_tiny_implies_not_distinct_and_brainy = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Tiny", [Variable("$x")])), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If there is someone who is distinct, then he is not tiny, and vice versa.
distinct_and_not_tiny_vice versa = Equivalence(Predicate("Distinct", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Tiny", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If there is someone who is not capable or not eager, then Kenneth is not distinct.
not_capable_or_not_eager_implies_not_distinct_kenneth = Implication(Disjunction(Negation(Predicate("Capable", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Constant("kenneth")]))

# Given premise: If someone is distinct, then he is both not capable and brainy.
distinct_and_not_capable_and_brainy = Implication(Predicate("Distinct", [Variable("$x")]), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Capable", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: Someone being eager is equivalent to being not distinct.
eager_equivalent_to_not_distinct = Equivalence(Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Distinct", [Variable("$x")]))

# Combine all premises and hypothesis into a single formula
combined_formula = Conjunction(
    premise,
    distinct_and_not_tiny,
    not_distinct_or_tiny,
    distinct_and_not_capable_and_brainy,
    not_dangerous_and_not_brainy_equivalent_to_not_capable,
    not_tiny_implies_not_distinct_and_brainy,
    distinct_and_not_tiny_vice versa,
    not_capable_or_not_eager_implies_not_distinct_kenneth,
    distinct_and_not_capable_and_brainy,
    eager_equivalent_to_not_distinct,
    hypothesis
)

# Final formula
final_formula = combined_formula

# Context: Benjamin is not distinct.

Predicate('Dangerous', ['arnold'])

# Context: Arnold is not eager.
Constant('arnold')
Negation(Predicate('Eager', ['arnold']))

# Context: Hale is brainy.
Constant('hale')
Predicate('Brainy', ['hale'])

# Context: Landon is dangerous.
Constant('landon')
Predicate('Dangerous', ['landon'])

# Context: Landon is brainy.
Constant('landon')
Predicate('Brainy', ['landon'])

# Context: Landon is not capable.
Constant('landon')
Negation(Predicate('Capable', ['landon']))

# Context: Arnold is not tiny.
Constant('arnold')
Negation(Predicate('Tiny', ['arnold']))

# Context: Hale is not capable.
Constant('hale')
Negation(Predicate('Capable', ['hale']))

# Context: Hale is tiny.
Constant('hale')
Predicate('Tiny', ['hale'])

# Context: If there is someone who is either not capable or not eager, then Kenneth is not distinct.
ExistentialQuantification(
    Disjunction(Negation(Predicate('Capable', ['x'])), Negation(Predicate('Eager', ['x']))),
    'x',
    Predicate('NotDistinct', ['kenneth'])
)

# Context: Someone being both not dangerous and not brainy is equivalent to being not capable.
Equivalence(
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate('Dangerous', ['x'])), Negation(Predicate('Brainy', ['x']))),
    Negation(Predicate('Capable', ['x']))
)

# Context: As long as someone is not tiny, he is not distinct and brainy.
Implication(
    Negation(Predicate('Tiny', ['x'])),
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate('Distinct', ['x'])), Predicate('Brainy', ['x']))
)

# Context: Hale being not dangerous and Arnold being distinct imply that Arnold is not brainy.
Implication(
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate('Dangerous', ['hale'])), Predicate('Distinct', ['arnold'])),
    Negation(Predicate('Brainy', ['arnold']))
)

# Context: If there is someone who is eager, then Landon is not distinct.
ExistentialQuantification(
    Predicate('Eager', ['x']),
    'x',
    Predicate('NotDistinct', ['landon'])
)

# Context: As long as someone is capable and brainy, he is distinct and not eager.
Implication(
    Conjunction(Predicate('Capable', ['x']), Predicate('Brainy', ['x'])),
    Conjunction(Predicate('Distinct', ['x']), Negation(Predicate('Eager', ['x'])))
)

# Context: If there is someone who is both brainy and not distinct, then Landon is tiny.
ExistentialQuantification(
    Conjunction(Predicate('Brainy', ['x']), Negation(Predicate('Distinct', ['x']))),
    'x',
    Predicate('Tiny', ['landon'])
)

# Context: Kenneth is capable if and only if Kenneth is not brainy.
Equivalence(
    Predicate('Capable', ['kenneth']),
    Negation(Predicate('Brainy', ['kenneth']))
)

# Context: If someone is distinct, then he is not tiny, and vice versa.
Equivalence(
    Predicate('Distinct', ['x']),
    Negation(Predicate('Tiny', ['x']))
)

# Context: If Hale is not distinct, then Hale is eager.
Implication(
    Negation(Predicate('Distinct', ['hale'])),
    Predicate('Eager', ['hale'])
)

# Context: If someone is distinct, then he is both not capable and brainy.
Implication(
    Predicate('Distinct', ['x']),
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate('Capable', ['x'])), Predicate('Brainy', ['x']))
)

# Context: Someone being eager is equivalent to being not distinct.
Equivalence(
    Predicate('Eager', ['x']),
    Negation(Predicate('Distinct', ['x']))
)
```

=== [Bin 9] Sample 936 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Kenneth is brainy.
hypothesis = Predicate("Brainy", [Constant("Kenneth")])

# Given premise: Kenneth is capable if and only if Kenneth is not brainy.
premise = Equivalence(Predicate("Capable", [Constant("Kenneth")]), Negation(Predicate("Brainy", [Constant("Kenneth")]))) 

# Since Kenneth is brainy, according to the premise, Kenneth cannot be capable.
contradiction = Implication(hypothesis, Negation(premise))

# The final formula representing the contradiction.
formula = End(contradiction)
```

# Given hypothesis: Oscar is responsible and Axel is shy once knowing that Monroe is not distinct.
# We need to derive this from the given premises.

# Step-by-step derivation:

# Premise 1: If Monroe is distinct, then Garret is eager, and vice versa.
# This can be written as:
# ∀x (Distinct(x) ↔ Eager(Garret))

# Premise 2: If there is someone who is either eager or dramatic, then Garret is not distinct.
# This can be written as:
# ∀x (Eager(x) ∨ Dramatic(x) → ¬Distinct(Garret))

# Premise 3: Someone being not dramatic is equivalent to being responsible.
# This can be written as:
# ∀x (¬Dramatic(x) ↔ Responsible(x))

# Premise 4: If there is someone who is both dramatic and not eager, then Garret is not responsible.
# This can be written as:
# ∀x (Dramatic(x) ∧ ¬Eager(x) → ¬Responsible(Garret))

# Premise 5: If there is at least one person who is shy, then Monroe is not eager and Archibald is dramatic.
# This can be written as:
# ∃x Shy(x) → ¬Eager(Monroe) ∧ Dramatic(Archibald)

# Premise 6: If someone is fancy, then he is both eager and not distinct.
# This can be written as:
# ∀x (Fancy(x) → Eager(x) ∧ ¬Distinct(x))

# Premise 7: If someone is not eager, then he is both dramatic and not distinct.
# This can be written as:
# ∀x (¬Eager(x) → Dramatic(x) ∧ ¬Distinct(x))

# Premise 8: If someone is fancy or he is distinct, then he is dramatic.
# This can be written as:
# ∀x (Fancy(x) ∨ Distinct(x) → Dramatic(x))

# Premise 9: Axel is distinct if and only if Garret is not responsible.
# This can be written as:
# ∀x (Distinct(Axel) ↔ ¬Responsible(Garret))

# Premise 10: If there is at least one person who is either not distinct or responsible, then Axel is not shy and Garret is dramatic.
# This can be written as:
# ∃x (¬Distinct(x) ∨ Responsible(x)) → ¬Shy(Axel) ∧ Dramatic(Garret)

# Conclusion: Oscar is responsible and Axel is shy once knowing that Monroe is not distinct.
# This can be written as:
# ¬Distinct(Monroe) → Responsible(Oscar) ∧ Shy(Axel)

# Let's formalize this conclusion using the given premises.

# First, let's express the conclusion in terms of the premises:
# ¬Distinct(Monroe) → Responsible(Oscar) ∧ Shy(Axel)

# Using the contrapositive, we can rewrite it as:
# ¬(Responsible(Oscar) ∧ Shy(Axel)) → Distinct(Monroe)

# Simplifying the left side:
# ¬Responsible(Oscar) ∨ ¬Shy(Axel) → Distinct(Monroe)

# Now, let's check if this can be derived from the premises.

# From Premise 5, we know:
# ∃x Shy(x) → ¬Eager(Monroe) ∧ Dramatic(Archibald)

# Since we don't have information about whether anyone is shy, we cannot directly use this premise.

# However, we can use the fact that if Monroe is not distinct, then Garret must be responsible (from Premise 1).

# And if Garret is responsible, then Axel must be not responsible (from Premise 9).

# But we need to show that if Monroe is not distinct, then Oscar is responsible and Axel is shy.

# Since we don't have direct information about Oscar and Axel's statuses based on Monroe's status alone, we cannot derive the conclusion directly from the given premises.

# Therefore, the conclusion is not necessarily true based on the given premises.

# The final answer is:
None

```python
# Given premise: Bernard is drab.
bernardIsDrab = Predicate("Drab", [Constant("bernard")])

# Given premise: Atwood is not dangerous.
atwoodIsNotDangerous = Predicate("NotDangerous", [Constant("atwood")])

# Given premise: Bernard is square.
bernardIsSquare = Predicate("Square", [Constant("bernard")])

# Given premise: Gabriela is square.
gabrielaIsSquare = Predicate("Square", [Constant("gabriela")])

# Given premise: As long as someone is not capable, he is bad-tempered and not square.
notCapableImpliesBadTemperedAndNotSquare = Implication(
    Negation(Predicate("Capable", [Variable("$x")])),
    Conjunction(Predicate("BadTempered", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Square", [Variable("$x")])))
)

# Given premise: Someone who is not efficient is always both drab and capable.
notEfficientImpliesDrabAndCapable = Implication(
    Negation(Predicate("Efficient", [Variable("$x")])),
    Conjunction(Predicate("Drab", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Capable", [Variable("$x")]))
)

# Given premise: Bernard is drab if and only if Gabriela is efficient.
bernardDrabIffGabrielaEfficient = Equivalence(
    bernardIsDrab,
    Predicate("Efficient", [Constant("gabriela")])
)

# Given premise: Someone being both not dangerous and not capable is equivalent to being not square.
notDangerousAndNotCapableEquivalentToNotSquare = Equivalence(
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Dangerous", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Capable", [Variable("$x")]))),
    Negation(Predicate("Square", [Variable("$x")]))
)

# Given premise: Someone who is not efficient is always both drab and capable.
notEfficientImpliesDrabAndCapable = Implication(
    Negation(Predicate("Efficient", [Variable("$x")])),
    Conjunction(Predicate("Drab", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Capable", [Variable("$x")]))
)

# Combine all the premises into a single formula
combinedFormula = Conjunction(
    bernardIsDrab,
    atwoodIsNotDangerous,
    bernardIsSquare,
    gabrielaIsSquare,
    notCapableImpliesBadTemperedAndNotSquare,
    notEfficientImpliesDrabAndCapable,
    bernardDrabIffGabrielaEfficient,
    notDangerousAndNotCapableEquivalentToNotSquare,
    notEfficientImpliesDrabAndCapable
)

# The final formula
formula = End(combinedFormula)
```

# Given the premise context, let's derive the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis.

# Step-by-step derivation:

# 1. **Premise Analysis**:
   - Gavin is doubtful.
   - Atwood is fragile.
   - Roger is mysterious.
   - Hanley is not mysterious.
   - Daisy is mysterious.
   - Roger is different.
   - Roger is fragile.
   - Daisy is not fragile.
   - Abbott is not clean.
   - Hanley is doubtful.
   - Daisy is different.
   - Carrick is not bitter.
   - If there is someone who is either not doubtful or fragile, then Carrick is not mysterious.
   - It can be concluded that Carrick is clean and Daisy is different once knowing that Abbott is not bitter.
   - Someone being clean is equivalent to being not bitter.
   - If someone is not bitter and clean, then he is doubtful, and vice versa.
   - If there is someone who is different, then Abbott is bitter.
   - Daisy being doubtful implies that Daisy is not fragile and Roger is clean.
   - It can be concluded that Atwood is mysterious once knowing that Atwood is not doubtful and Roger is not bitter.
   - It can be concluded that Abbott is bitter once knowing that Daisy is not mysterious.
   - If everyone is different or not bitter, then Abbott is not clean.
   - Someone is not clean if and only if he is doubtful.
   - If someone is both not fragile and not doubtful, then he is not bitter.
   - It can be concluded that Roger is clean and Carrick is not fragile once knowing that Carrick is mysterious.

# 2. **Formulating the Hypothesis**:
   - The hypothesis we need to formulate is based on the conclusion derived from the premises.

# 3. **Conclusion**:
   - From the premises, we know that if Carrick is mysterious, then Carrick is not fragile and Roger is clean.

# 4. **First-Order Logic Formula**:
   - We need to express this conclusion in first-order logic.

# Final Formula:
ExistentialQuantification(
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Mysterious", [Constant("carrick")]),
        Conjunction(
            Negation(Predicate("Fragile", [Constant("carrick")])),
            Predicate("Clean", [Constant("roger")])
        )
    ), "carrick"
)

# This formula states that there exists at least one Carrick who is mysterious, and if such a Carrick exists, then Carrick is not fragile and Roger is clean.
```
