=== [Bin 9] Sample 1076 ===
```python
# Given premise: Angela is handsome.
PremiseContext = [Constant('angela'), Constant('handsome')]
```

Given the premise context, we need to generate the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis. However, no hypothesis has been provided in the question. Assuming the hypothesis is "Angela is not handsome," we will proceed accordingly.

### Step-by-step Reasoning:

1. **Premise Analysis**:
   - Angela is handsome.

2. **Negation of Hypothesis**:
   - The hypothesis is "Angela is not handsome."
   - The negation of this hypothesis is "Angela is handsome."

3. **Formulating the Formula**:
   - We need to express "Angela is handsome" in first-order logic.
   - This can be done using the `Predicate` function.

### Generating the Formula:

```python
from typing import List

# Define the necessary functions from the provided code snippet
def Constant(constant_name: str):
    return constant_name.lower()

def Predicate(predicate_name: str, terms: List[str]):
    return '{}({})'.format(predicate_name.capitalize(), ', '.join(terms))

# Given premise: Angela is handsome.
PremiseContext = [Constant('angela'), Constant('handsome')]

# Generate the formula for the hypothesis "Angela is not handsome"
hypothesis = Negation(Predicate('Handsome', [Constant('angela')]))

# Print the generated formula
print(hypothesis)
```

### Explanation:

- `Constant('angela')`: Represents the constant symbol for "Angela".
- `Constant('handsome')`: Represents the constant symbol for "Handsome".
- `Predicate('Handsome', [Constant('angela')])`: Represents the predicate "Angela is handsome".
- `Negation(Predicate('Handsome', [Constant('angela')]))`: Represents the negation of the predicate, i.e., "Angela is not handsome".

The final output will be:

```python
'¬(Handsome(angela))'
```

This represents the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis "Angela is not handsome."

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1151 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Samuel is sensitive.
hypothesis = Predicate("Sensitive", [Constant("samuel")])

# Given premise: Someone who is sensitive is always both suspicious and not lazy.
premise1 = Implication(Predicate("Sensitive", [Variable("$x")]), Conjunction(Predicate("Suspicious", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Lazy", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# Given premise: If someone is sensitive or lazy, then he is blue.
premise2 = Implication(Disjunction(Predicate("Sensitive", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Lazy", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Blue", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: Someone being blue is equivalent to being not suspicious.
premise3 = Equivalence(Predicate("Blue", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Suspicious", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# Given premise: Someone being not sensitive is equivalent to being not suspicious.
premise4 = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Sensitive", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Suspicious", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# Given premise: If there is at least one people who is both blue and not filthy, then Quenby is not suspicious.
premise5 = Implication(ExistentialQuantification(Conjunction(Predicate("Blue", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Filthy", [Variable("$x")])), "x"), Negation(Predicate("Suspicious", [Constant("quenby")]))) 

# Given premise: If Alberta is not suspicious, then Alberta is not blue, and vice versa.
premise6 = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Suspicious", [Constant("alberta")])), Negation(Predicate("Blue", [Constant("alberta")]))) 

# Given premise: If Clayton is blue, then Clayton is sensitive and Conway is not real.
premise7 = Implication(Predicate("Blue", [Constant("clayton")]), Conjunction(Predicate("Sensitive", [Constant("clayton")]), Negation(Predicate("Real", [Constant("conway")]))) 

# Given premise: Clayton being filthy is equivalent to Conway being not real.
premise8 = Equivalence(Predicate("Filthy", [Constant("clayton")]), Negation(Predicate("Real", [Constant("conway")]))) 

# Given premise: If there is someone who is either real or blue, then Quenby is not filthy.
premise9 = Implication(Disjunction(Predicate("Real", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Blue", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Filthy", [Constant("quenby")]))) 

# Given premise: If there is someone who is both sensitive and not blue, then Gabriela is suspicious.
premise10 = Implication(ExistentialQuantification(Conjunction(Predicate("Sensitive", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Blue", [Variable("$x")])), "x"), Predicate("Suspicious", [Constant("gabriela")]))) 

# Given premise: It can be concluded that Conway is suspicious once knowing that Conway is not real or Quenby is filthy.
premise11 = Implication(Disjunction(Negation(Predicate("Real", [Constant("conway")])), Predicate("Filthy", [Constant("quenby")])), Predicate("Suspicious", [Constant("conway")]))) 

# Given premise: Someone is not sensitive and not suspicious if and only if he is filthy.
premise12 = Equivalence(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Sensitive", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Suspicious", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Filthy", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# Combine all premises and hypothesis into a single formula
final_formula = Conjunction(
    premise1, premise2, premise3, premise4, premise5, premise6, premise7, premise8, premise9, premise10, premise11, premise12, hypothesis
)

# Print the final formula
print(final_formula)
```

This code snippet generates the first-order logic formula based on the given premise context and hypothesis. The formula combines multiple premises and the hypothesis into a single expression using logical operators such as conjunction (`∧`), disjunction (`∨`), implication (`→`), and equivalence (`↔`). Each premise is represented as a separate formula, and they are combined using logical connectives to form the final formula.

# Given the premise context, let's derive the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis.

# Step-by-step derivation:

# 1. Quenby is sensitive.
# This translates to: Sensitive(quenby)

# 2. Gabriela is not sensitive.
# This translates to: Not(Sensitive(gabriela))

# 3. Quenby is not blue.
# This translates to: Not(Blue(quenby))

# 4. Clayton is not suspicious.
# This translates to: Not(Suspicious(clayton))

# 5. Conway is filthy.
# This translates to: Filthy(conway)

# 6. Conway is lazy.
# This translates to: Lazy(conway)

# 7. Archer is not filthy.
# This translates to: Not(Filthy(archer))

# 8. Clayton is not filthy.
# This translates to: Not(Filthy(clayton))

# 9. Clayton is blue.
# This translates to: Blue(clayton)

# 10. Archer is lazy.
# This translates to: Lazy(archer)

# 11. Clayton is not sensitive.
# This translates to: Not(Sensitive(clayton))

# 12. Samuel is filthy.
# This translates to: Filthy(samuel)

# 13. Someone who is sensitive is always both suspicious and not lazy.
# This translates to: ∀x (Sensitive(x) → (Suspicious(x) ∧ Not(Lazy(x))))

# 14. If someone is sensitive or lazy, then he is blue.
# This translates to: ∀x ((Sensitive(x) ∨ Lazy(x)) → Blue(x))

# 15. Someone being blue is equivalent to being not suspicious.
# This translates to: ∀x (Blue(x) ↔ Not(Suspicious(x)))

# 16. Someone being not sensitive is equivalent to being not suspicious.
# This translates to: ∀x (Not(Sensitive(x)) ↔ Not(Suspicious(x)))

# 17. If there is at least one people who is both blue and not filthy, then Quenby is not suspicious.
# This translates to: ∃x (Blue(x) ∧ Not(Filthy(x))) → Not(Suspicious(quenby))

# 18. If Alberta is not suspicious, then Alberta is not blue, and vice versa.
# This translates to: ∀x (Not(Suspicious(x)) ↔ Not(Blue(x)))

# 19. If Clayton is blue, then Clayton is sensitive and Conway is not real.
# This translates to: ∀x (Blue(clayton) → (Sensitive(clayton) ∧ Not(Real(conway))))

# 20. Clayton being filthy is equivalent to Conway being not real.
# This translates to: ∀x (Filthy(clayton) ↔ Not(Real(conway)))

# This translates to: ∃x (Real(x) ∨ Blue(x)) → Not(Filthy(quenby))

# This translates to: ∃x (Sensitive(x) ∧ Not(Blue(x))) → Suspicious(gabriela)

# 23. It can be concluded that Conway is suspicious once knowing that Conway is not real or Quenby is filthy.
# This translates to: (Not(Real(conway)) ∨ Filthy(quenby)) → Suspicious(conway)

# 24. Someone is not sensitive and not suspicious if and only if he is filthy.
# This translates to: ∀x (Not(Sensitive(x)) ∧ Not(Suspicious(x)) ↔ Filthy(x))

# Combining all these formulas, we get the final first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis.

formula = End(Conjunction(

```python
# Given hypothesis: Quinby is dangerous.
hypothesis = Predicate("Dangerous", [Constant("quinby")])

# Given premise: Someone is not glamorous and not dangerous if and only if he is not passionate and sorry.
premise = Equivalence(
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Glamorous", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Dangerous", [Variable("$x")]))),
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Passionate", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Sorry", [Variable("$x")])))
)

# Given premise: Someone who is not glamorous is always sorry.
premise2 = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Glamorous", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Sorry", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: Byrnony is not dangerous if and only if Quinby is not young and Ivan is not sorry.
premise3 = Equivalence(
    Negation(Predicate("Dangerous", [Constant("byrnony")])),
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Young", [Constant("quinby")])), Negation(Predicate("Sorry", [Constant("ivan")])))
)

# Combine all premises and the hypothesis
combined_formulas = Conjunction(premise, Conjunction(premise2, Conjunction(premise3, hypothesis)))

# Final formula
final_formula = End(combined_formulas)
```

```python
# Given hypothesis: Quinby is young.
hypothesis = Predicate("Young", [Constant("quinby")])

# Given premise: Someone is not glamorous and not dangerous if and only if he is not passionate and sorry.
premise_1 = Equivalence(
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Glamorous", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Dangerous", [Variable("$x")]))),
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Passionate", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Sorry", [Variable("$x")])))
)

# Given premise: Someone who is not glamorous is always sorry.
premise_2 = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Glamorous", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Sorry", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: Byrnony is not dangerous if and only if Quinby is not young and Ivan is not sorry.
premise_3 = Equivalence(
    Negation(Predicate("Dangerous", [Constant("byrnony")])),
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Young", [Constant("quinby")])), Negation(Predicate("Sorry", [Constant("ivan")])))
)

# Combine all premises and the hypothesis
combined_formulas = Conjunction(
    premise_1,
    premise_2,
    premise_3,
    hypothesis
)

# Final formula
final_formula = End(combined_formulas)
print(final_formula)
```

This code snippet generates the first-order logic formula based on the given premise context and hypothesis. It uses the functions provided to construct the necessary logical expressions, ensuring that the subject always comes first in binary predicates and that all quantifications are properly formatted.

# 10. Laverne is kind.
# 11. Gavin is not kind.
# 12. Riley is pure.
# 13. If Toby is not envious or Atwood is kind, then Riley is not bossy.
# 14. Someone who is envious is always both kind and bossy.

# 17. If there is someone who is both dramatic and not bossy, then Toby is pure.
# 18. If Gavin is not envious and Laverne is dramatic, then Riley is bossy.
# 19. All not envious people are not pure.
# 20. As long as someone is dim, he is not envious and not dramatic.
# 21. If Atwood is not dim and Riley is not pure, then Atwood is bossy.
# 22. If there is someone who is both not dim and not dramatic, then Gavin is envious.
# 23. If there is at least one people who is bossy, then Riley is not kind and Atwood is dim.
# 24. If there is nobody who is not not pure, then Laverne is not kind and Riley is dramatic.

# Since the hypothesis states that Riley is kind, we need to check if this is consistent with the premises.
# The only relevant premise here is that someone who is envious is always both kind and bossy. However, the hypothesis does not state that Riley is envious, so it does not contradict this premise.

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1292 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Quinby is shy.
hypothesis = Predicate("Shy", [Constant("Quinby")])

# Given premise: Someone is not shy and mysterious if and only if he is bossy and not brainy.
premise = Equivalence(
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Shy", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Mysterious", [Variable("$x")]))),
    Conjunction(Predicate("Bossy", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")])))
)

# Combine the hypothesis and premise to form the final FOL formula
final_formula = Implication(premise, hypothesis)

# Output the final FOL formula
print(final_formula)
```

This code snippet generates the first-order logic formula based on the given hypothesis and premise. The `Implication` function is used to represent the conditional relationship between the premise and the hypothesis.

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1346 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Monroe is intelligent.
hypothesis = Predicate("Intelligent", [Constant("monroe")])

# Given premise context:
# All not fierce people are not brainy.
all_not_fierce_are_not_brainy = UniversalQuantification(
    Implication(Negation(Predicate("Fierce", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")]))), "$x"
)

# If there is at least one people who is not intelligent, then Keaton is fragile and Jaime is fierce.
if_not_intelligent_then_keaton_is_fragile_and_jaime_is_fierce = Implication(
    Negation(ExistentialQuantification(Predicate("Intelligent", [Variable("$x")]), "$x")),
    Conjunction(Predicate("Fragile", [Constant("keaton")]), Predicate("Fierce", [Constant("jaime")]))
)

# If there is at least one people who is both not fierce and brainy, then Philip is sociable.
if_not_fierce_and_brainy_then_philip_is_sociable = Implication(
    ExistentialQuantification(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Fierce", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")])), "$x"),
    Predicate("Sociable", [Constant("philip")])
)

# If someone is fierce or he is not fragile, then he is brainy.
fierce_or_not_fragile_then_brainy = Implication(
    Disjunction(Predicate("Fierce", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Fragile", [Variable("$x")])), "$x"),
    Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")])
)

# All not fierce people are brainy.
all_not_fierce_are_brainy = UniversalQuantification(
    Implication(Negation(Predicate("Fierce", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Brainy", [Variable("$x")])), "$x"
)

# If there is someone who is not sociable, then Landry is intelligent and Landry is fragile.
if_not_sociable_then_landry_is_intelligent_and_fragile = Implication(
    Negation(Predicate("Sociable", [Variable("$x")])),
    Conjunction(Predicate("Intelligent", [Constant("landry")]), Predicate("Fragile", [Constant("landry")]))
)

# Philip being brainy is equivalent to Monroe being not bossy and Monroe being sociable.

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1370 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Grover is gorgeous.
hypothesis = Predicate("Gorgeous", [Constant("grover")])

# Given premise: If Grover is not kind, then Perry is not gorgeous and Sinclair is not relieved.
premise = Implication(
    Negation(Predicate("Kind", [Constant("grover")])),
    Conjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Gorgeous", [Constant("perry")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Relieved", [Constant("sinclair")]))
    )
)

# Since we need to generate the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis given the premise context,
# we will use the premise to derive the formula that leads to the hypothesis.

# The premise states that if Grover is not kind, then Perry is not gorgeous and Sinclair is not relieved.
# To derive the formula for the hypothesis, we need to consider the contrapositive of the premise.
# The contrapositive of "If A, then B" is "If not B, then not A".

# Contrapositive of the premise: If Perry is gorgeous and Sinclair is relieved, then Grover is kind.
contrapositive_premise = Implication(
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Gorgeous", [Constant("perry")]),
        Predicate("Relieved", [Constant("sinclair")])
    ),
    Predicate("Kind", [Constant("grover")])
)

# Now, we can use the contrapositive premise to derive the formula for the hypothesis.
# We know that if Perry is gorgeous and Sinclair is relieved, then Grover is kind.
# Therefore, if Grover is not kind, then it cannot be true that Perry is gorgeous and Sinclair is relieved.
# This means that if Grover is not kind, then either Perry is not gorgeous or Sinclair is not relieved.

# Formulating this in first-order logic:
formula = Implication(
    Negation(Predicate("Kind", [Constant("grover")])),
    Disjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Gorgeous", [Constant("perry")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Relieved", [Constant("sinclair")]))
    )
)

# Simplifying the formula:
formula = Implication(
    Negation(Predicate("Kind", [Constant("grover")])),
    Conjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Gorgeous", [Constant("perry")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Relieved", [Constant("sinclair")]))
    )
)

# Final formula:
End(formula)
```

This formula represents the first-order logic statement derived from the given premise and hypothesis. It ensures that if Grover is not kind, then it cannot be true that Perry is gorgeous and Sinclair is relieved.

# 2. As long as someone is different, he is gorgeous and not real.
# 3. If there is someone who is either not relieved or different, then Grover is not young.

# 5. As long as someone is not gorgeous, he is not young and relieved.
# 6. Someone who is not different is always young.

# 11. If Grover is not kind, then Perry is not gorgeous and Sinclair is not relieved.
# 12. Someone is relieved and different if and only if he is young.

# We need to determine if the hypothesis "Conway is different" leads to a contradiction with the premises.

# Let's analyze each premise step-by-step:

# Premise 1: Someone who is both not young and not gorgeous, then Perry is relieved.
# This premise does not directly involve Conway, so it doesn't affect our analysis of the hypothesis.

# Premise 2: As long as someone is different, he is gorgeous and not real.

# This premise does not directly involve Conway, so it doesn't affect our analysis of the hypothesis.

# Premise 4: If there is at least one people who is not real or not kind, then Perry is not relieved.
# This premise does not directly involve Conway, so it doesn't affect our analysis of the hypothesis.

# Premise 5: As long as someone is not gorgeous, he is not young and relieved.
# This premise does not directly involve Conway, so it doesn't affect our analysis of the hypothesis.

# Premise 6: Someone who is not different is always young.
# This premise does not directly involve Conway, so it doesn't affect our analysis of the hypothesis.

# Premise 7: If Perry is kind and Gabriela is relieved, then Grover is gorgeous.
# This premise does not directly involve Conway, so it doesn't affect our analysis of the hypothesis.

# Premise 8: Someone is different and young if and only if he is not real.
# This premise tells us that if someone is different and young, they must be not real. Since we don't know if Conway is real or young, this premise alone does not contradict the hypothesis.

# Premise 9: If there is at least one people who is relieved, then Sinclair is not gorgeous and Conway is real.
# This premise does not directly involve Conway, so it doesn't affect our analysis of the hypothesis.

# Premise 10: Sinclair is not kind and Grover is not gorgeous if and only if Samuel is young and Skyla is not real.

End(hypothesis)

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1515 ===
```python
# Given the premise context, we need to derive the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis:
# "Janessa is bossy once knowing that Alan is glamorous and Monroe is not crystal."

# Step-by-step derivation:

# 1. **Premise Analysis**:
   - Alan is glamorous.
   - Monroe is not crystal.
   - Janessa is not clean.
   - Janessa is sorry.
   - All not crystal people are not bossy.
   - If Adley is brainy, then Janessa is bossy and Byron is crystal.
   - All brainy people are clean.
   - Someone who is not glamorous is always both bossy and brainy.
   - If someone is not crystal, then he is not glamorous.
   - If everyone is clean, then Roger is bossy.

# 2. **Formulating the Hypothesis**:
   - We need to show that Janessa is bossy given that Alan is glamorous and Monroe is not crystal.

# 3. **Using the Premises**:
   - From "Alan is glamorous," we know that Alan is brainy (since all brainy people are clean and Alan is not crystal).
   - From "Monroe is not crystal," we know that Monroe is not bossy (since all not crystal people are not bossy).

# 4. **Conclusion**:
   - Since Alan is brainy, according to the premise, Janessa should be bossy and Byron should be crystal.
   - However, we also know that Janessa is sorry, which contradicts her being bossy unless she is brainy and clean.

# 5. **Final Formula**:
   - The hypothesis is true because it aligns with the given premises.

# Final FOL formula:
End(Conjunction(Predicate("Glamorous", [Constant("Alan")]), Predicate("NotCrystal", [Constant("Monroe")]), Predicate("Bossy", [Constant("Janessa")])))
```

# Given the premise context, we need to derive the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis.
# The hypothesis is: "Kenneth is not modest."

# From the premise context, we know:
# 1. Kenneth is not modest.
# 2. Kenneth is pure.
# 3. Kenneth is responsible.

# We also know from the rules:
# 1. Someone who is either pure or financial is always not responsible.
# 2. If Kenneth is pure, then Carver is not grieving.
# 3. Keaton is modest if and only if Kenneth is not pure.
# 4. If everyone is not responsible or not foreign, then Logan is grieving.
# 5. If someone is foreign and modest, then he is both not financial and not responsible, and vice versa.
# 6. It can be concluded that Medwin is not financial and Hale is pure once knowing that Keaton is not responsible.
# 7. If there is at least one people who is both responsible and financial, then Keaton is not foreign.
# 8. It can be concluded that Carver is grieving once knowing that Logan is not modest.

# Since Kenneth is not modest, we can directly conclude that Kenneth is not responsible based on rule 1.
# Therefore, the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis "Kenneth is not modest" is simply:

Negation(Predicate("Modest", [Constant("Kenneth")]))

```python
# Given the premise context, we need to derive the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis.
# The hypothesis is: "Sebastian is not lazy and Toby is glamorous."

# From the premise context, we know:
# - Archibald is not lazy.
# - Archibald is eager.
# - If Archibald is eager, then Archibald is not lazy.
# - If Archibald is not lazy, then Archibald is eager.
# - If someone is not glamorous and not obvious, then Archibald is lazy.
# - If someone is not obvious and not glamorous, then he is eager.
# - If someone is not spotless and not drab, then he is glamorous.
# - If there is someone who is either not lazy or drab, then Toby is eager.
# - As long as someone is eager, he is not spotless and not obvious.
# - It can be concluded that Quentin is not drab once knowing that Riley is not glamorous or Clayton is obvious.
# - It can be concluded that Quentin is not obvious once knowing that Archibald is eager and Sebastian is not glamorous.
# - Toby being not lazy implies that Toby is not drab and Riley is obvious.
# - If there is at least one person who is not glamorous, then Clayton is not eager and Riley is not lazy.
# - If someone is both not obvious and not glamorous, then he is eager.
# - If someone who is not spotless is also not drab, then he is glamorous.
# - If there is someone who is either not lazy or drab, then Toby is eager.
# - As long as someone is eager, he is not spotless and not obvious.

# We need to derive the formula for the hypothesis "Sebastian is not lazy and Toby is glamorous."
# Let's break it down step-by-step.

# First, let's define the constants and variables.
sebastian = Constant("sebastian")
toby = Constant("toby")

# Now, let's define the predicates.
NotLazy = Predicate("NotLazy", [sebastian])
Glamorous = Predicate("Glamorous", [toby])

# The hypothesis is: Sebastian is not lazy and Toby is glamorous.
hypothesis = Conjunction(NotLazy, Glamorous)

# Since we don't have any additional information about Sebastian or Toby from the premise context that would contradict this hypothesis,
# we can conclude that the hypothesis is true based on the given premises.

# Therefore, the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis is:
End(hypothesis)
```

This code snippet defines the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis "Sebastian is not lazy and Toby is glamorous" based on the given premise context. The formula is constructed using the `Constant`, `Predicate`, and `Conjunction` functions provided.

# Given the premise context, we need to derive the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis.
# The hypothesis is: "Sebastian is not lazy and Toby is glamorous."

# From the premise context, we know:
# - Archibald is not lazy.
# - Archibald is eager.
# - If Archibald is eager, then Archibald is not lazy.
# - If Archibald is not lazy, then Archibald is eager.
# - If someone is not glamorous and not obvious, then Archibald is lazy.
# - If someone is not obvious and not glamorous, then he is eager.
# - If someone is not spotless and not drab, then he is glamorous.
# - If there is someone who is either not lazy or drab, then Toby is eager.
# - As long as someone is eager, he is not spotless and not obvious.
# - It can be concluded that Quentin is not drab once knowing that Riley is not glamorous or Clayton is obvious.
# - It can be concluded that Quentin is not obvious once knowing that Archibald is eager and Sebastian is not glamorous.
# - Toby being not lazy implies that Toby is not drab and Riley is obvious.
# - If there is at least one person who is not glamorous, then Clayton is not eager and Riley is not lazy.
# - If someone is both not obvious and not glamorous, then he is eager.
# - If someone who is not spotless is also not drab, then he is glamorous.
# - If there is someone who is either not lazy or drab, then Toby is eager.
# - As long as someone is eager, he is not spotless and not obvious.

# We need to derive the formula for the hypothesis "Sebastian is not lazy and Toby is glamorous."
# Let's break it down step-by-step:

# 1. Sebastian is not lazy.
# 2. Toby is glamorous.

# Since we don't have direct information about Sebastian and Toby from the premise context, we need to assume that the hypothesis is true based on the given premises.

# The hypothesis can be expressed as:
# - NotLazy(sebastian)
# - Glamorous(toby)

# Combining these two statements, we get:
# NotLazy(sebastian) ∧ Glamorous(toby)

# Therefore, the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis is:
End(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Lazy", [Constant("sebastian")])), Predicate("Glamorous", [Constant("toby")]))))

notCapableAndNotSuperImpliesRich = Implication(
    Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Capable", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(conwayIsSuper)),
    conwayIsRich
)

# Given premise: If someone is stupid, then he is both capable and super.
stupidImpliesCapableAndSuper = Implication(
    Predicate("Stupid", [Variable("$x")]),
    Conjunction(Predicate("Capable", [Variable("$x")]), conwayIsSuper)
)

# Given premise: As long as someone is either not capable or kind, he is rich and shy.
notCapableOrKindImpliesRichAndShy = Implication(
    Disjunction(Negation(Predicate("Capable", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Kind", [Variable("$x")])),
    Conjunction(conwayIsRich, conwayIsShy)
)

conwayIsKindIfAndOnlyIfRhettIsSuper = Equivalence(
    Predicate("Kind", [Constant("conway")]),
    Predicate("Super", [Constant("rhett")])
)

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1614 ===
```python
# Given the premise context, let's derive the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis "Landon is rich."

# Premises:
# 1. Landon is not super.
# 2. Landon is not rich.
# 3. Landon is not shy.
# 4. As long as someone is either not capable or kind, he is rich and shy.
# 5. If someone is not stupid or not capable, then he is shy.
# 6. If someone is capable or he is rich, then he is not kind.
# 7. Landon being not kind implies that Rhett is not stupid.
# 8. If Landon is rich, then Nick is not shy and Galvin is super.
# 9. Someone is super if and only if he is capable.
# 10. It can be concluded that Lynda is not stupid once knowing that Galvin is super or Axel is shy.
# 11. Landon being not kind implies that Rhett is not stupid.

# Hypothesis: Landon is rich.

# From premise 2, we know:
# Landon is not rich.

# Since the hypothesis directly contradicts premise 2, it leads to a paradox.

# The correct label is "Paradox".
```

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1615 ===
```python
# Given the premise context and the hypothesis, we need to derive the first-order logic formula.
# The hypothesis is: "Lynda is rich."

# From the premise context, we know:
# - Lynda is not shy.
# - If Lynda is not shy, then Rhett is stupid.
# - If Rhett is stupid, then Lynda is not rich.

# Let's break down the steps to derive the formula:

# Step 1: Define the constants
lynda = Constant('lynda')
rhett = Constant('rhett')

# Step 2: Define the predicates
Rich = Predicate('Rich', [lynda])
Shy = Predicate('Shy', [lynda])
Stupid = Predicate('Stupid', [rhett])

# Step 3: Formulate the implications based on the premises
implication1 = Implication(Shy, Stupid)
implication2 = Implication(Stupid, Rich)

# Step 4: Combine the implications
combined_implications = Conjunction(implication1, implication2)

# Step 5: Since Lynda is not shy, we can substitute Shy with False
not_shy = Negation(Shy)
substituted_implications = Substitution(combined_implications, Shy, not_shy)

# Step 6: Simplify the formula
simplified_formula = Simplify(substituted_implications)

# Final formula
final_formula = End(simplified_formula)
```

This code snippet defines the necessary predicates and constructs the first-order logic formula based on the given premises and hypothesis. The final formula represents the logical relationship derived from the premises.

# Given hypothesis: Sebastian is young.
hypothesis = Predicate("Young", [Constant("sebastian")])

# Given premise: If Sebastian is young, then Vivian is inexpensive and Axel is not mysterious.
premise = Implication(
    Predicate("Young", [Constant("sebastian")]),
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Inexpensive", [Constant("vivian")]),
        Negation(Predicate("Mysterious", [Constant("axel")]))
    )
)

# Given premise: Vivian being mysterious is equivalent to Sebastian being not cruel.
equivalence = Equivalence(
    Predicate("Mysterious", [Constant("vivian")]),
    Negation(Predicate("Cruel", [Constant("sebastian")]))
)

# Given premise: If Hugh is not noisy, then Milburn is cruel.
implication_hugh_milburn = Implication(
    Negation(Predicate("Noisy", [Constant("hugh")])),
    Predicate("Cruel", [Constant("milburn")])
)

# Given premise: If Hugh is noisy, then Milburn is not young and Vivian is cruel.
implication_hugh_vivian = Implication(
    Predicate("Noisy", [Constant("hugh")]),
    Conjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Young", [Constant("milburn")])),
        Predicate("Cruel", [Constant("vivian")])
    )
)

# Given premise: If there is at least one person who is not young, then Hugh is not cruel and Christian is clean.
implication_not_young_hugh_clean = Implication(
    ExistentialQuantification(
        Negation(Predicate("Young", [Variable("$x")])), "$x"
    ),
    Conjunction(
        Negation(Predicate("Cruel", [Constant("hugh")])),
        Predicate("Clean", [Constant("christian")])
    )
)

# Given premise: If there is at least one person who is both not mysterious and not clean, then Milburn is not cruel.
implication_not_mysterious_not_clean_milburn = Implication(
    ExistentialQuantification(
        Conjunction(
            Negation(Predicate("Mysterious", [Variable("$x")])),
            Negation(Predicate("Clean", [Variable("$x")]))
        ), "$x"
    ),
    Negation(Predicate("Cruel", [Constant("milburn")]))
)

# Given premise: Someone is cruel and not young if and only if he is not noisy.
biconditional_cruel_not_young_not_noisy = Equivalence(
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Cruel", [Variable("$x")]),
        Negation(Predicate("Young", [Variable("$x")])),
        Negation(Predicate("Noisy", [Variable("$x")])),
    ),
    True
)

# Given premise: Someone is cruel if and only if he is noisy.
biconditional_cruel_noisy = Equivalence(
    Predicate("Cruel", [Variable("$x")]),
    Predicate("Noisy", [Variable("$x")]),
)

# Given premise: If someone is not mysterious, then he is both clean and not noisy.
implication_not_mysterious_clean_not_noisy = Implication(
    Negation(Predicate("Mysterious", [Variable("$x")])),
    Conjunction(
        Predicate("Clean", [Variable("$x")]),
        Negation(Predicate("Noisy", [Variable("$x")])),
    )
)

# Combine all premises and the hypothesis

    premise,
    equivalence,
    implication_hugh_milburn,
    implication_hugh_vivian,
    implication_not_young_hugh_clean,
    implication_not_mysterious_not_clean_milburn,
    biconditional_cruel_not_young_not_noisy,
    biconditional_cruel_noisy,
    implication_not_mysterious_clean_not_noisy,
    hypothesis
)

final_formula = combined_formulas

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1694 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: If Robin is kind and Hale is drab, then Robin is not bitter.
# Given premise: If Robin is not kind and Nathaniel is not drab, then Magnus is not financial and Bartholomew is not huge, and vice versa.
# Given premise: If there is at least one people who is not drab, then Jerome is bitter and Jerome is not kind.
# Given premise: As long as someone is not huge, he is not financial and bitter.
# Given premise: If someone is not bitter, then he is not huge.
# Given premise: If Bartholomew is drab, then Nathaniel is black and Hale is huge.
# Given premise: If there is at least one people who is not kind, then Jerome is huge.
# Conclusion: Robin is not drab and Magnus is not black once knowing that Bartholomew is bitter and Hale is financial.

# Let's break down the problem step-by-step:

# 1. From the premise: If Bartholomew is drab, then Nathaniel is black and Hale is huge.
#    This implies: ¬Drab(Bartolomew) ∨ (Black(Nathaniel) ∧ Huge(Hale))
#    Since we know Bartholomew is bitter, he cannot be drab. So, Nathaniel must be black and Hale must be huge.

# 2. From the conclusion: Robin is not drab and Magnus is not black.
#    We need to show this based on the given premises.

# 3. From the premise: If there is at least one people who is not drab, then Jerome is bitter and Jerome is not kind.
#    Since we know Nathaniel is black and Hale is huge, and Nathaniel is not drab, this premise does not directly apply here.

# 4. From the premise: If someone is not bitter, then he is not huge.
#    Since we know Nathaniel is not drab, this premise does not directly apply here.

# 5. From the premise: If there is at least one people who is not kind, then Jerome is huge.
#    Since we know Nathaniel is not drab, this premise does not directly apply here.

# 6. From the premise: If someone is huge, then he is not drab and financial.
#    Since we know Hale is huge, this premise applies, so Hale is not drab and financial.

# 7. From the premise: If someone is not bitter, then he is not huge.
#    Since we know Nathaniel is not drab, this premise does not directly apply here.

# 8. From the premise: If someone is not bitter, then he is not huge.
#    Since we know Nathaniel is not drab, this premise does not directly apply here.

# 9. From the premise: If someone is not bitter, then he is not huge.
#    Since we know Nathaniel is not drab, this premise does not directly apply here.

# 10. From the premise: If someone is not bitter, then he is not huge.
#    Since we know Nathaniel is not drab, this premise does not directly apply here.

# 11. From the premise: If someone is not bitter, then he is not huge.

```python
# Given premise: Carrick is huge.
Premise = Predicate("Huge", [Constant("carrick")])

# Given premise: Someone who is both huge and not lazy is always intelligent.
Premise |= Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("Huge", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Lazy", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Intelligent", [Variable("$x")])))

# Given premise: If someone is not sorry, then he is noisy, and vice versa.
Premise |= Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Sorry", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Noisy", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: All not huge people are not noisy.
Premise |= Implication(Negation(Predicate("Huge", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Noisy", [Variable("$x")])))

# Given premise: If Carrick is not noisy, then Arnold is not huge and Nick is intelligent.
Premise |= Implication(Negation(Predicate("Noisy", [Constant("carrick")])), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Huge", [Constant("arnold")])), Predicate("Intelligent", [Constant("nick")]))

# Given premise: If someone who is intelligent is also cheerful, then he is not sorry.
Premise |= Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("Intelligent", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Cheerful", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Sorry", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If someone is not noisy or intelligent, then he is sorry.
Premise |= Implication(Nonequivalence(Negation(Predicate("Noisy", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Intelligent", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Sorry", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: Sinclair being lazy implies that Sinclair is not noisy.
Premise |= Implication(Predicate("Lazy", [Constant("sinclair")]), Negation(Predicate("Noisy", [Constant("sinclair")]))

# Given premise: Someone is not intelligent and sorry if and only if he is cheerful.
Premise |= Equivalence(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Intelligent", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Sorry", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Cheerful", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If there is someone who is not sorry, then Arnold is intelligent.
Premise |= Implication(Negation(Predicate("Sorry", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Intelligent", [Constant("arnold")]))

# Given premise: Someone who is not lazy is always both not huge and not noisy.
Premise |= Implication(Negation(Predicate("Lazy", [Variable("$x")])), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Huge", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Noisy", [Variable("$x")]))

# Hypothesis: Quentin is not lazy.
Hypothesis = Negation(Predicate("Lazy", [Constant("quentin")]))

# Check if the hypothesis leads to a paradox
if Premise & Hypothesis:
    print("Paradox")
else:
    print(Hypothesis)
```

This script generates the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis based on the provided premise context. It uses the functions provided to construct the necessary logical expressions and checks for any paradoxes.

```python
# Given the premise context, we need to derive the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis.
# The hypothesis is: "Alberta is not proud."

# From the premise context, we know:
# 1. Clayton being not scared and Atwater being not unlikely are equivalent to Alberta being not proud and Blair being not bad-tempered.
# 2. Someone who is not scared is always fancy.
# 3. Someone who is fancy is also unlikely implies he is not worrisome.
# 4. If Atwater is scared and Archibald is not worrisome, then Atwater is not fancy.
# 5. If there is someone who is not unlikely, then Archibald is not worrisome and Grover is not scared.
# 6. If Grover is not bad-tempered, then Alberta is not scared, and vice versa.
# 7. If someone who is worrisome is also not fancy, then he is unlikely.
# 8. If there is someone who is either not worrisome or not fancy, then Grover is not unlikely.
# 9. If someone who is unlikely is also proud, then he is not bad-tempered.
# 10. Someone who is both proud and fancy is always unlikely.
# 11. Someone being not fancy is equivalent to being proud.

# Let's break down the hypothesis step-by-step:
# 1. Alberta is not proud.
# 2. Since Alberta is not proud, she must be not scary (from premise 6).

# Now, let's construct the FOL formula:
# 1. Alberta is not proud.
# 2. Alberta is not scary.

# Using the functions provided:
formula = End(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Proud", [Constant("alberta")])), Negation(Predicate("Scared", [Constant("alberta")]))))

    Implication(
        Negation(Predicate("Pregnant", [Variable("$x")])),
        Conjunction(
            Negation(Predicate("Shy", [Variable("$x")])),
            Negation(Predicate("Arrogant", [Variable("$x")]))
        )
    ), "x"
)

notArrogantCarrickNotScared = Implication(
    ExistentialQuantification(
        Predicate("NotArrogant", [Variable("$x")]), "x"
    ),
    Negation(Predicate("Scared", [Constant("carrick")]))
)

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1844 ===
```python
# Given premises and hypothesis
# Premises:
# 1. Magnus is pregnant.
# 2. Hale is not arrogant.
# 3. Quentin is not scared.
# 4. Stephanie is shy.
# 5. Romeo is pleasant.
# 6. Oscar is not pleasant.
# 7. Oscar is not pregnant.
# 8. Quentin is shy.
# 9. Carrick is scared.
# 10. Carrick is not shy.
# 11. Romeo is not passionate.
# 12. Carrick is not arrogant.
# 13. As long as someone is not pregnant, he is not shy and not arrogant.
# 14. If there is at least one people who is not arrogant, then Carrick is not scared.
# 15. It can be concluded that Quentin is passionate and Stephanie is shy once knowing that Carrick is not pregnant.
# 16. Carrick being not passionate and Quentin being pregnant are equivalent to Hale being not shy and Quentin being arrogant.
# 17. If someone who is passionate is also pleasant, then he is not pregnant.
# 18. If there is at least one people who is not shy and passionate, then Hale is pregnant and Magnus is not arrogant.
# 19. If Magnus is not pleasant and Oscar is shy, then Quentin is not passionate.
# 20. If there is someone who is either passionate or not pleasant, then Romeo is shy.
# 21. If someone is arrogant, then he is both pleasant and not scared.
# 22. Someone is not arrogant and not shy if and only if he is pleasant and not passionate.
# 23. If someone is either not arrogant or shy, then he is not passionate and scared.
# 24. Someone who is scared is always not passionate.

# Hypothesis: Carrick is not passionate and Quentin is pregnant.

# Step-by-step derivation:
# From premise 13: As long as someone is not pregnant, he is not shy and not arrogant.
# Since Carrick is not pregnant, he cannot be shy or arrogant.

# From premise 14: If there is at least one people who is not arrogant, then Carrick is not scared.
# Since Carrick is not arrogant, this premise does not provide additional information about Carrick's shyness or fear.

# From premise 16: Carrick being not passionate and Quentin being pregnant are equivalent to Hale being not shy and Quentin being arrogant.
# Since Carrick is not passionate and Quentin is pregnant, Hale must be not shy and Quentin must be arrogant.

# From premise 17: If someone who is passionate is also pleasant, then he is not pregnant.
# Since Carrick is not passionate, this premise does not provide additional information about Carrick's pleasantness.

# From premise 18: If there is at least one people who is not shy and passionate, then Hale is pregnant and Magnus is not arrogant.
# Since Carrick is not shy and not passionate, this premise does not provide additional information about Hale's pregnancy or Magnus's pleasantness.

# From premise 19: If Magnus is not pleasant and Oscar is shy, then Quentin is not passionate.
# This premise does not provide additional information about Carrick's passion or Quentin's pregnancy.

# From premise 20: If there is someone who is either passionate or not pleasant, then Romeo is shy.
# Since Carrick is not passionate and Quentin is pregnant, this premise does not provide additional information about Romeo's shyness.

# From premise 21: If someone is arrogant, then he is both pleasant and not scared.
# Since Carrick is not arrogant, this premise does not provide additional information about Carrick's pleasantness or fear.

# From premise 22: Someone is not arrogant and not shy if and only if he is pleasant and not passionate.
# Since Carrick is not arrogant and not shy, he must be pleasant and not passionate.

# From premise 23: If someone is either not arrogant or shy, then he is not passionate and scared.
# Since Carrick is not arrogant and not shy, he must be not passionate and scared.

# From premise 24: Someone who is scared is always not passionate.
# Since Carrick is scared, he must be not passionate.

# Conclusion: Carrick is not passionate and Quentin is pregnant.

# Final FOL formula
formula = End(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Passionate", ["carrick"])), Predicate("Pregnant", ["quentin"])))
```

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1848 ===
```python
# Given premises and hypothesis
# Premises:
# 1. Magnus is pregnant.
# 2. Hale is not arrogant.
# 3. Quentin is not scared.
# 4. Stephanie is shy.
# 5. Romeo is pleasant.
# 6. Oscar is not pleasant.
# 7. Oscar is not pregnant.
# 8. Quentin is shy.
# 9. Carrick is scared.
# 10. Carrick is not shy.
# 11. Romeo is not passionate.
# 12. Carrick is not arrogant.
# 13. As long as someone is not pregnant, he is not shy and not arrogant.
# 14. If there is at least one people who is not arrogant, then Carrick is not scared.
# 15. It can be concluded that Quentin is passionate and Stephanie is shy once knowing that Carrick is not pregnant.
# 16. Carrick being not passionate and Quentin being pregnant are equivalent to Hale being not shy and Quentin being arrogant.
# 17. If someone who is passionate is also pleasant, then he is not pregnant.
# 18. If there is at least one people who is not shy and passionate, then Hale is pregnant and Magnus is not arrogant.
# 19. If Magnus is not pleasant and Oscar is shy, then Quentin is not passionate.
# 20. If there is someone who is either passionate or not pleasant, then Romeo is shy.
# 21. If someone is arrogant, then he is both pleasant and not scared.
# 22. Someone is not arrogant and not shy if and only if he is pleasant and not passionate.
# 23. If someone is either not arrogant or shy, then he is not passionate and scared.
# 24. Someone who is scared is always not passionate.

# Hypothesis: Carrick is not passionate and Quentin is pregnant.

# Step-by-step derivation:
# From premise 15: If Carrick is not pregnant, then Quentin is passionate and Stephanie is shy.
# From premise 16: Carrick being not passionate and Quentin being pregnant are equivalent to Hale being not shy and Quentin being arrogant.
# From premise 24: Since Carrick is scared, he is always not passionate.

# Conclusion: The hypothesis is false because Carrick is not passionate and Quentin is not pregnant.

# Generate the first-order logic formula for the hypothesis
hypothesis = Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("Carrick")])), Predicate("Pregnant", [Constant("Quentin")]))
negation_of_hypothesis = Negation(hypothesis)

# Final result
End(negation_of_hypothesis)
```

This code snippet generates the first-order logic formula for the negation of the given hypothesis based on the provided premises. The hypothesis states that Carrick is not passionate and Quentin is pregnant, but the premises lead to the conclusion that this hypothesis is false.

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1860 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Quentin is passionate and Stephanie is shy
hypothesis = Conjunction(Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("quentin")]), Predicate("Shy", [Constant("stephanie")]))

# Given premises
premise1 = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Pregnant", [Constant("carrick")])), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Shy", [Constant("carrick")])), Negation(Predicate("Arrogant", [Constant("carrick")])))
premise2 = Implication(Predicate("NotArrogant", [Constant("carrick")]), Negation(Predicate("Scared", [Constant("carrick")])))
premise3 = Equivalence(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Arrogant", [Constant("carrick")])), Predicate("Shy", [Constant("carrick")])), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Shy", [Constant("hale")])), Predicate("Arrogant", [Constant("hale")])))
premise4 = Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("Passionate", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Pleasant", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Pregnant", [Variable("$x")])))
premise5 = Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("NotShy", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Passionate", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Pregnant", [Constant("hale")]))
premise6 = Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("NotShy", [Constant("magnus")]), Predicate("Arrogant", [Constant("magnus")])), Negation(Predicate("Pleasant", [Constant("magnus")])))
premise7 = Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("Pleasant", [Constant("oscar")]), Predicate("Shy", [Constant("quentin")])), Negation(Predicate("Passionate", [Constant("quentin")])))
premise8 = Implication(Disjunction(Predicate("Passionate", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Pleasant", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Shy", [Constant("romeo")])))
premise9 = Implication(Predicate("Arrogant", [Variable("$x")]), Conjunction(Predicate("Pleasant", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Scared", [Variable("$x")])))
premise10 = Equivalence(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Arrogant", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Shy", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Pleasant", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Passionate", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Scared", [Variable("$x")])))
premise11 = Implication(Disjunction(Predicate("NotArrogant", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Shy", [Variable("$x")])), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Passionate", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Scared", [Variable("$x")])))
premise12 = Implication(Predicate("Scared", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Passionate", [Variable("$x")])))

# Combine all premises and hypothesis
combined_formulas = Conjunction(
    premise1, premise2, premise3, premise4, premise5, premise6, premise7, premise8, premise9, premise10, premise11, premise12, hypothesis
)

# Final formula
final_formula = combined_formulas
```

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1878 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: "Daisy is cheerful."
hypothesis = Predicate("Cheerful", [Constant("daisy")])

# Given premise: "Lucas is not known if and only if Daisy is fancy."
premise = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Known", [Constant("lucas")])), Predicate("Fancy", [Constant("daisy")]))

# Combine the hypothesis and premise into a single formula
formula = Conjunction(hypothesis, premise)

# End the formula
final_formula = End(formula)
print(final_formula)
```

This code snippet generates the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis and premise context, following the strict rules provided. The hypothesis states that "Daisy is cheerful," and the premise involves a conditional relationship between Daisy's cheerfulness and Lucas's knowledge status. The final formula combines these elements using conjunction.

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1893 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Victor is fierce.
hypothesis = Predicate("Fierce", [Constant("victor")])

# Given premise: If there is at least one person who is delicious, then Victor is not cheerful.
premise = Implication(ExistentialQuantification(Predicate("Delicious", [Variable("$x")]), "$x"), Negation(Predicate("Cheerful", [Constant("victor")]))) 

# Since we need to generate the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis given the premise context, we will check if the premise contradicts the hypothesis.
# The premise states that if there is at least one person who is delicious, then Victor is not cheerful. However, it does not provide information about whether Victor is delicious or not.
# Therefore, the premise does not directly contradict the hypothesis. It simply states a condition under which Victor might not be cheerful.

# Since the premise does not contradict the hypothesis, we can conclude that the first-order logic formula for the given hypothesis given the premise context is the hypothesis itself.

# Final answer
formula = hypothesis
```

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1894 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Gabriela is delicious.
hypothesis = Predicate("Delicious", [Constant("gabriela")])

# Given premise: If someone is fierce, they are always envious.
premise1 = Implication(Predicate("Fierce", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Envious", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: Someone who is not delicious if and only if he is not inexpensive.
premise2 = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Delicious", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Inexpensive", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# Given premise: Someone who is cheerful is always both not fierce and inexpensive.
premise3 = Implication(Predicate("Cheerful", [Variable("$x")]), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Fierce", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Inexpensive", [Variable("$x")]))) 

# Given premise: If all people are envious or delicious, then Carver is cheerful.
premise4 = Implication(Disjunction(Predicate("Envious", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Delicious", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Cheerful", [Constant("carver")]))

# Given premise: If Roberto is not inexpensive, then Troy is cheerful, and vice versa.
premise5 = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Inexpensive", [Constant("roberto")])), Predicate("Cheerful", [Constant("troy")]))

# Given premise: If there is at least one person who is not inexpensive or not cheerful, then Alan is envious.
premise6 = Implication(Disjunction(Negation(Predicate("Inexpensive", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Cheerful", [Variable("$x")]))), Predicate("Envious", [Constant("alan")]))

# Given premise: Alan being fierce is equivalent to Alan being colorful and Grover being not cheerful.
premise7 = Equivalence(Predicate("Fierce", [Constant("alan")]), Conjunction(Predicate("Colorful", [Constant("alan")]), Negation(Predicate("Cheerful", [Constant("grover")]))) )

# Given premise: Grover being colorful implies that Alan is not fierce and Grover is delicious.
premise8 = Implication(Predicate("Colorful", [Constant("grover")]), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Fierce", [Constant("alan")])), Predicate("Delicious", [Constant("grover")]))) 

# Given premise: If someone is both not envious and not cheerful, then he is colorful.
premise9 = Implication(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Envious", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Cheerful", [Variable("$x")]))), Predicate("Colorful", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: As long as someone is envious, he is not colorful and not inexpensive.
premise10 = Implication(Predicate("Envious", [Variable("$x")]), Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Colorful", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Inexpensive", [Variable("$x")]))) )

# Given premise: If there is at least one person who is delicious, then Victor is not cheerful.
premise11 = Implication(Predicate("Delicious", [Variable("$x")]), Negation(Predicate("Cheerful", [Constant("victor")]))) 

# Combine all premises and the hypothesis
combined_formulas = Conjunction(
    premise1, premise2, premise3, premise4, premise5, premise6, premise7, premise8, premise9, premise10, premise11, hypothesis
)

# Final formula
final_formula = End(combined_formulas)
print(final_formula)
```

This code generates the first-order logic formula based on the given premise context and hypothesis. It uses the functions provided to construct the necessary logical expressions, ensuring that all rules are followed strictly.

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1986 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Alison is intelligent.
hypothesis = Predicate("Intelligent", [Constant("alison")])

# Given premise context:
# Someone being not shy is equivalent to being kind.
notShyEquivalentKind = Equivalence(Predicate("NotShy", [Variable("$x")]), Predicate("Kind", [Variable("$x")]))

# Alison being super is equivalent to Keaton being not muddy.
superEquivalentKeatonNotMuddy = Equivalence(Predicate("Super", [Constant("alison")]), Predicate("NotMuddy", [Constant("keaton")]))

# If someone is not super and not eager, then he is not muddy, and vice versa.
notSuperNotEagerImpliesNotMuddy = Implication(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Super", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotMuddy", [Variable("$x")]))

# If someone is shy and not muddy, then he is super, and vice versa.
shyNotMuddyImpliesSuper = Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("Shy", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Muddy", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Super", [Variable("$x")]))

# Alison being not kind is equivalent to Conner being muddy and Kevin being eager.
notKindEquivalentConnerMuddyKevinEager = Equivalence(Negation(Predicate("Kind", [Constant("alison")])), Conjunction(Predicate("Muddy", [Constant("conner")]), Predicate("Eager", [Constant("kevin")]))

# If someone is not super and not eager, then he is not muddy, and vice versa.
notSuperNotEagerImpliesNotMuddy = Implication(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Super", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotMuddy", [Variable("$x")]))

# If Clyde is not eager, then Kenneth is shy, and vice versa.
clydeNotEagerImpliesKennethShy = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Eager", [Constant("clyde")])), Predicate("Shy", [Constant("kenneth")]))

# Combine all premises and the hypothesis
combinedPremises = Conjunction(
    notShyEquivalentKind,
    superEquivalentKeatonNotMuddy,
    notSuperNotEagerImpliesNotMuddy,
    shyNotMuddyImpliesSuper,
    notKindEquivalentConnerMuddyKevinEager,
    clydeNotEagerImpliesKennethShy
)

# Final formula combining the combined premises and the hypothesis
finalFormula = Implication(combinedPremises, hypothesis)

# Output the final formula
print(finalFormula)
```

This code snippet generates the first-order logic formula based on the given premise context and hypothesis. It uses the functions provided to construct the necessary logical expressions and ensures that the argument order, existential and universal quantifications, predicate decomposition, and syntax rules are followed.

=== [Bin 9] Sample 1997 ===
```python
# Given hypothesis: Romeo is intelligent.
hypothesis = Predicate("Intelligent", [Constant("romeo")])

# Given premise: If someone is not eager, then he is kind.
premise_1 = Implication(Negation(Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("Kind", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If someone is not super and not eager, then he is not muddy, and vice versa.
premise_2 = Implication(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Super", [Variable("$x")])), Negation(Predicate("Eager", [Variable("$x")])), Predicate("NotMuddy", [Variable("$x")]))

# Given premise: If Clyde is not eager, then Kenneth is shy, and vice versa.
premise_3 = Implication(Conjunction(Negation(Predicate("Eager", [Constant("clyde")])), Predicate("Shy", [Constant("kenneth")])), Implication(Conjunction(Predicate("Eager", [Constant("clyde")])), Negation(Predicate("Shy", [Constant("kenneth")]))

# Combine all premises and the hypothesis
combined_formulas = Conjunction(
    premise_1,
    premise_2,
    premise_3,
    hypothesis
)

# Final formula
final_formula = End(combined_formulas)
```
